Classification: UNCLASSIFIED
Caveats: NONE

I was asked to complete a life cycle cost analysis comparing a boiler/chiller
system vs a geothermal system with a water to water heat pump.  I have all
the factors I need except the illusive O&M costs. (I have fuel cost,
installation cost, equipment cost....)

What I have done at this point is to contact the company which will actually
be doing the O&M.  (I went straight to the horse's mouth) but the campus only
has 1 building with a geo-thermal system and it is only 1 year old.  I think
if I can get a ratio comparing the O&M costs of a boiler/chiller vs
geothermal, I'd be done.  

Something as simple as "A geothermal system on average costs x% less in O&M
costs compared to a boiler/chiller system...." and somebody to site as to the
value of 'x'.

John Eurek PE, LEED AP

Classification: UNCLASSIFIED
Caveats: NONE

be lower so data slightly favorable to chiller-boiler.:

GHP Maint Cost = 2.13$/yr/sq ft
Water Cooled Chiller w Gas Fired Hot Water Boiler $6.07/yr/Sq Ft

John, 

You may also consult the following paper for more details:

Martin, M.A., Madgett, M.G., and Hughes, P.J. "Comparing Maintenance Costs of Geothermal Heat Pumps Systems with Other HVAC Systems: Preventive Maintenance Actions and Total Maintenance Costs", ASHRAE Transactions: v106-1, 2000.

It is available, e.g., from http://www.geoenergyservices.com/resources/1-2.pdf.

Demba NDIAYE

From hwasilow at gsd.harvard.edu  Tue Jul  5 15:42:42 2011
From: hwasilow at gsd.harvard.edu (Holly W. Samuelson)
Date: Tue, 5 Jul 2011 18:42:42 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] July 12 Symposium on Weather and Building Simulation
Message-ID: <CANSEwuv-RjNhv+Xg5Ojo29caXBkhHDL8sCBd0dABgf7PHPuSCg@mail.gmail.com>

Dear Bldg-Sim Group,
You are invited to the Symposium on Weather and Building Simulation,
organized by IBPSA (International Building Performance Simulation
Association) -USA Boston Chapter. This event is Part 2 of 3 in our "Big
Uglies" series, investigating the major unknowns in energy modeling.

**

*Symposium on Weather and Building Simulation *

*July 12, 2011 7:30-9:30pm*

*Room 111 Harvard Graduate School of Design, 48 Quincy Street, Cambridge, MA
*

*With Live Webcast*



The evening will include presentations and an open discussion on current
best practices. Presenters include:

·         Drury Crawley of Bentley Systems

·         Charles Khuen of Weather Analytics

·         Seth Holmes of Harvard Graduate School of Design

Topics include:

·         Design conditions and different types of hourly weather data

·         Where to obtain this data

·         The limitations of each type

·         What to do when there's no nearby data

·         The Climate Forecast System Reanalysis (CFSR) dataset

·         Simulation with future climate-change data



Refreshments will be provided. For more information on IBPSA-USA Boston,
visit: http://ibpsa-boston.com/ For directions to the event please visit:
http://www.gsd.harvard.edu/admissions/visiting/ Participates unable to make
it to Cambridge can join us via the internet. Details to follow. However, in
order to create a lively discussion, we request that local participants join
us in person if possible.**



*PLEASE RSVP by sending an email with the subject "RSVP In-Person" or "RSVP
Remote" to **ibpsa.boston at gmail.com* <ibpsa.boston at gmail.com>*.*  Please
forward this invitation to anyone you think may be interested.

Best,
Holly W. Samuelson
IBPSA-USA Boston

From Matthew.Herman at BuroHappold.com  Tue Jul  5 16:03:37 2011
From: Matthew.Herman at BuroHappold.com (Matthew Herman)
Date: Wed, 6 Jul 2011 00:03:37 +0100
Subject: [Bldg-sim] job posting
Message-ID: <143E1AEC203D2440830EC9332C21D6890192A811@ex-be02.burohappold.com>


Job Title:          Building Analytics Team Leader

 
Ref:                        
 
Location:             Chicago and New York 
 
Buro Happold is an international multidisciplinary consulting
engineering practice that succeeds through the individual and
collaborative skills of its people. Our offices are expanding and we are
looking for a team leader to head our Building Analytics Group in
Chicago and New York.
 
Job Purpose:
We are looking for a motivated team leader to head our building
analytics group.  This group is responsible for computational analysis
related to energy efficiency, building physics, life cycle costs
analysis and other analytical design services.  The successful
candidate(s) will have experience in design and simulation of mechanical
systems including heating, cooling, heat rejection, air distribution,
ventilation, building automation and controls.  Applicants should have a
strong understanding of modelling and analysis techniques related to
building systems, facades, and controls as well as the use of DOE2, IES
VE, or Energy Plus. PE licence with an experience in low energy building
engineering is a plus. The work will include executing and reviewing
building simulations, providing strategic design advice to client teams,
and assisting in design documentation and specification on a wide
variety of building types and scales.   The ideal candidate will also be
required to perform analysis, which will range from simple desktop
studies to comprehensive life cycle assessments of a variety of energy
conversation measures.  
 
Qualifications:
*         Master's degree related to Building Science and/or Mechanical
Engineering
*         Energy Modelling Software experience (eg: DOE-2, eQuest,
EnergyPlus, IES, TRNSYS)
*         Working knowledge of ASHRAE standard 90.1 and related energy
standards and modelling mythologies
 
Qualifications (a plus):
*         Professional Engineer in Illinois and/or New York
*         High-performance Building Design Professional Certified from
ASHRAE
*         Building Energy Modelling Professional (BEMP)
*         Certified Energy Managers (CEM)
*         Building Automation Controls Experience
*         Commissioning and retro commissioning experience
*         Work experience
*         Demonstrated project management and leadership skills
 
Requirements:
*	Field visits to various sites in area and region (potential
around the US) 
*	Perform energy analysis and prepare report/presentation
deliverables 
*	Manage client relationships for energy efficiency products
*	Team leadership skills and organizational techniques 
*	Develop design strategies, conceptual and schematic options
*	Preliminary cost-estimating and calculation of payback periods 
*	Fluent in written and spoken English
 
 
To apply for this position visit our website
http://www.burohappold.com/BH/OPP_SearchCurrentVacancies.aspx.  
Please submit resume only with salary expectations.
 
No recruitment consultants or agencies please. 
 
 
Matthew Herman
Office Director

Full time positions with Buro Happold are currently available in Chicago
and New York.
 
Buro Happold is a leading engineering consulting firm with over 1800
employees worldwide in 28 offices. Our North America offices are located
in New York City, Chicago, Los Angles, and San Francisco.  Buro Happold
is currently expanding its Computational Simulation & Analysis
.  This team's role focuses on providing design advice relating to
building performance, environmental quality, and energy issues.
Currently there are positions available for qualified individuals with
experience in the following areas.
 
*         Mechanical system design and analysis 
*         Computational Fluid Dynamics 
*         Building forensics
*         Building physics 
*         Post Occupancy Monitoring 
 
 
Job Description:
Buro Happold is seeking qualified individuals with experience in design
and analysis of mechanical systems including heating, cooling, heat
rejection, air distribution, ventilation, building automation and
controls. Applicants should have a strong understanding of modelling and
analysis techniques related to building systems and controls. PE licence
with an experience in low energy building engineering is a plus. The
work will include executing building simulations, providing strategic
design advice, and assisting in design documentation and specification
on a wide variety of low energy
Buildings.    
 
Responsibilities will include:
 
1.      Design advice on selection of equipment and controls for use in
building systems. 
 
2.      Computer modeling of the mechanical systems to understand the
energy and thermal comfort performance under various conditions and
design configurations.
 
3.      Sustainable design consulting through the incorporation of
energy efficient and renewable technologies.   
 
4.      Report formation and presenting of analysis results in a clear
and precise way to multidisciplinary design teams.

From abakshi at yrgsustainability.com  Wed Jul  6 13:41:32 2011
From: abakshi at yrgsustainability.com (Arpan Bakshi)
Date: Wed, 6 Jul 2011 16:41:32 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] JOB POSTING - Commissioning Agent / Energy Auditor
Message-ID: <CAHbW238wga2tRKZ9Ek88xRSyVZuJQCWErHwafDp+TpsvxCDhpQ@mail.gmail.com>

*Commissioning Agent / Energy Auditor*

Location: New York, NY

Position Type: Full Time



*POSITION DESCRIPTION*

YRG is seeking a Commissioning Agent and/or Energy Auditor to support our
green building work on new and existing building projects. For new
construction projects, the candidate will perform all tasks associated with
building energy system commissioning as specified by LEED, including
pre-functional systems review, functional performance testing, review of OPR
and BOD, and writing commissioning reports. For existing facility projects,
the candidate will conduct ASHARE Level I and II energy audits,
re-commissioning, and provide recommendations for cost-effective efficiency
improvements.



In addition, the candidate will provide general support to YRG staff
(including energy modelers, design assistance providers, operations and
maintenance consultants), to inform the strategic and technical direction of
projects, and optimize those projects relative to the client’s
sustainability goals. Our goal is to provide a seamless transition from
design and energy modeling, through commissioning and operations to help
ensure that sustainability goals are seen through to the long term.



Knowledge of the ASHRAE-90.1 energy codes, LEED green building rating
systems, and NYSERDA incentive programs are a must.



*QUALIFICATIONS *

The candidate should have a MEP engineering background, significant
experience (at least 3 years) working in a related field (building science,
MEP, etc.), completed at least five commissioning, energy audits, or
retro-commissioning projects as the lead on those projects, and a relevant
accreditation in the field (e.g. Certified Energy Manager, BCxA), etc.



The candidate should have knowledge of building energy systems (shell,
lighting, HVAC, controls) and efficiency measures. The ideal candidate will
have strong analytical skills, as well as strong communication skills, and
be both willing and able to support project teams in all aspects of building
design and operation.



Additional preferred technical knowledge and experience could include:

§  Renewable energy technologies and analytical models

§  Direct experience with the LEED-EB:OM and LEED-NC Rating Systems

§  Cost-benefit analyses

§  New Jersey, LIPA, CT, and federal rebates and incentives for energy
efficiency and renewable energy



Lastly, the successful candidate should exhibit strong time management,
organizational, and presentation skills; be flexible to changes in deadlines
and work focus; and be highly self-motivated. This position will require
working independently as well as closely with other staff members and
members of multiple client project teams.



*SALARY*

Salary is commensurate with experience.



*CONTACT *

If interested, please send a resume and a work sample (sample Cx plan, audit
report, etc.) to Alice Mogck at amogck at yrgsustainability.com

From oleksandra.onisko at ecovert.ca  Wed Jul  6 14:43:37 2011
From: oleksandra.onisko at ecovert.ca (Oleksandra Onisko)
Date: Wed, 6 Jul 2011 17:43:37 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Compartment units with DOAS (EE4)
Message-ID: <CAAkkLHEE=6gvSnmzGsXERmKc5gPjFneOH3OLVDTBFMOYgyujqQ@mail.gmail.com>

Hello,

We have a building with a dedicated fresh air unit supplying tempered air to
compartment units on each floor which feed VAV boxes. Right now, this is
being modelled as a separate VAV unit for each floor, with the heating and
cooling capacities, fan power, etc. from the DOAS being distributed and
added to the cooling capacities, fan power, etc. of the compartment units. I
am not sure that this is the correct way to represent this system in EE4 and
would welcome suggestions/advise on how to best model this system.

Cheers,

From ncaton at smithboucher.com  Wed Jul  6 14:56:20 2011
From: ncaton at smithboucher.com (Nick Caton)
Date: Wed, 6 Jul 2011 16:56:20 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] 90.1-2010 question
In-Reply-To: <000301cc3c24$4cbc7490$e6355db0$@buildingperformanceteam.com>
References: <005f01cc2062$98f9e6e0$caedb4a0$@net><20110706211238.5FBDB8DB01EE@zapata.dreamhost.com>
	<000301cc3c24$4cbc7490$e6355db0$@buildingperformanceteam.com>
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1ED1C@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

I have also taken those areas in the exceptions as the entire building's, though I'd agree it's not worded clearly enough to prevent various interpretations.  

I'm not an English major, but I think if you could find a grammar guru they'd be able to cite something to the following effect: When a sentence's subject and object is not made clear by any other means in the structure/word choice, the word closest to the verb is the object affected by that verb.

These exceptions to the mandated vestibule (varying by climate zone) seem generally logical to me.  While vestibules in many cases will save energy, at some point a vestibule of minimum dimensions becomes a significant fraction of cost and useable space for small buildings... Vestibules and other infiltration mitigation (revolving doors) cost money in and of themselves and probably generally do less for a tiny building relative to the savings to be had with larger buildings having heavier regular entry traffic.  That's not to say a vestibule is a BAD idea for small buildings - savings will be proportional to the entry traffic: a small McDonald's will get much more out of an entry vestibule than an equally-sized storage facility.

~Nick

NICK CATON, P.E.
SENIOR ENGINEER

Smith & Boucher Engineers
25501 west valley parkway, suite 200
olathe, ks 66061
direct 913.344.0036
fax 913.345.0617
www.smithboucher.com

From dse at grummanbutkus.com  Wed Jul  6 15:30:17 2011
From: dse at grummanbutkus.com (David Eldridge)
Date: Wed, 6 Jul 2011 17:30:17 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] 90.1-2010 question
In-Reply-To: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1ED1C@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
References: <005f01cc2062$98f9e6e0$caedb4a0$@net><20110706211238.5FBDB8DB01EE@zapata.dreamhost.com>
	<000301cc3c24$4cbc7490$e6355db0$@buildingperformanceteam.com>
	<FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1ED1C@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
Message-ID: <22496a912bd8be1f0eee12261c094c58@mail.gmail.com>

I always interpreted this the other way -- if the space adjacent to the
opening was small, then it was itself the vestibule.

I can see your point about adding cost to a small building -- but 1,000 ft2
is so small relative the scale of most buildings that it seems like this is
describing a space to me.

The wording would normally be "located in a climate zone" AND "that are less
than 1,000 ft2" if you meant for them to be both referring to the
buildings...unless there is a possibility that the entrance is not in the
same climate zone as the rest of the building ;)

Since "AND" is not there, if you delete the wording about the climate zone
from the sentence, it seems to clearly refer to the entrances and not the
building.

"Building...that are less than 1,000 ft2..."  ---> grammar guru rejects this
one

"Building entrances...that are less than 1,000 ft2..."  ---> grammar guru
celebrates, saves energy, and provides great IEQ!

So that said...anyone check the User's Manual?  This seems like a good item
to be included.  Maybe also a COMCheck envelope compliance report, I don't
recall the exact wording used there.  Perhaps it prints out the AHJ's
checklist with clearer wording.  I don't remember if this is included in
that checklist off the top of my head though.

David

The correct interpretation is buildings less than 1000 sf.   The example cited earlier of a small stand alone restaurant is valid.

Exception g is the exception for individual spaces.

From marcantoine.paquin at mra.qc.ca  Thu Jul  7 05:31:12 2011
From: marcantoine.paquin at mra.qc.ca (Marc-Antoine Paquin)
Date: Thu, 07 Jul 2011 08:31:12 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Compartment units with DOAS (EE4)
In-Reply-To: <CAAkkLHEE=6gvSnmzGsXERmKc5gPjFneOH3OLVDTBFMOYgyujqQ@mail.gmail.com>
References: <CAAkkLHEE=6gvSnmzGsXERmKc5gPjFneOH3OLVDTBFMOYgyujqQ@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <002801cc3ca1$c1deb120$459c1360$%paquin@mra.qc.ca>

Hi,

 

You should use Built-up VAV (Assemblé DAV) as your system deserving your
thermal zones.  As you said, you can use distinct system for each floor and
making sure that you distribute adequately the heating, cooling capacities,
fan power, etc. for each dummy systems. Then you will have to specify the
fan control in the supply fan label and indicate the maximum allowable air
supply for each zone.

 

Regards,

 

Marc-Antoine Paquin, ing.

 

cell. 514-402-9878

From carla.morton at autodesk.com  Thu Jul  7 11:48:11 2011
From: carla.morton at autodesk.com (Carla Morton (Temp))
Date: Thu, 7 Jul 2011 18:48:11 +0000
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Job Opening:  Green Buliding Science Software Engineer
Message-ID: <04F81C67D445AE4593C19E11CCD98B7601F18D@005-TK5MPN1-013.MGDADSK.autodesk.com>

Greetings:  Can you please share this job opening to your groups?
Thank you!
Carla Morton


Green Building Science Software Engineer
Req# 11000000GA
Autodesk: San Francisco/San Rafael, CA
Contact: Carla.morton at autodesk.com<mailto:Carla.morton at autodesk.com>



General Summary:

Do you want to work with one of the world's most admired software companies?  Do you want to have fun working with skilled and motivated teammates to develop the newest generation of energy analysis web service in the cloud?
Autodesk Green Building Studio provides web-based building energy analysis service to help architects and designers perform whole building analysis, optimize energy efficiency, and work toward carbon neutrality earlier in the design process. If you are an energy engineer with experience in software development in web-based or SaaS environment, this is the right opportunity for you.

Duties and Responsibilities:

  *   Work within an Agile and Scrum development team to extend the capabilities of Autodesk Green Building Studio web service as well as partner with Product Management, Subject Matter Expert in Building Science, Support, and Design to deliver high quality software throughout the development lifecycle.
  *   Design, prototype, develop, test, troubleshoot, and maintain source code of product development features.
  *   Prepare accurate implementation task lists/time estimates and deliver development assignments per functional specification or user story defined by the feature or scrum team.
  *   Play an important role in establishing both short and long term validation engineering strategy with the focus on building energy analysis results.
  *   Research and study relevant building scientific reports, and ASHARE journals and handworks to support updating our in-house HVAC, building, construction materials that are the centerpieces of our building energy analysis web service.
  *   Act as the Subject Matter Expert (SME) in development team to work with the Product Owner(s) and Product Design to understand and help refine the functional and non-functional requirements.
  *   Distill user stories and test cases from disparate sources, and research/analysis of existing legacy architecture and software to develop automated tests, make enhancements, and/or resolve defects and performance issues.
  *   Investigate and, as necessary, prototype technologies and algorithms relating to the task.
  *   Conduct and participate in peer code reviews with own and other teams.
  *   Provide mentorship and guidance to junior engineer and remote development team, help management team to increase the whole team's efficiency.

Requirements:

  *   A bachelor's degree or higher in engineering, or equivalent experience.
  *   3-4 years of experience in building energy field.
  *   Experience with building energy modeling tools like DOE2, Energyplus, and eQuest.
  *   2-3 years development and/or test experience in web-based or SaaS software development. Experience in Amazon and Azure cloud computing is a big plus.
  *   Working knowledge of XML, XSLT, SOAP, C#, ASP.NET, MVC, YUI, jQuery, SQL Server, and Stored Procedures.
  *   Good 3D geometry, math, and computer graphics programming skill.
  *   Good understanding of the software development lifecycle and experience with one or more products through all phases of the software development life cycle.
  *   Good oral and written communication skills.
  *   Proven ability to solve diverse and complex problems, with superior analytical, troubleshooting and debugging skills.  Thorough attention to detail.
  *   Ability to quickly learn a large body of existing code.
  *   Highly motivated, and a self-starter, with the ability to work efficiently with minimal supervision.

  *   Experience with Agile development methodologies, with Scrum a big plus.
  *   Experience with working in distributed teams.
  *   Knowledge and experience with Internet technologies application delivery, and protocols.

explain which avenue to take to my boss?  I am currently thinking new
building, but if it was existing then that helps me even more.

The baselines for the existing portion are what is there now.

 

The baselines for the new portion (addition) are ASHRAE definitions.

 

This should help considerably in your overall energy efficiency gains.

 

It all comes down to % new vs. % existing, on how much you can gain.

strategies for saving energy later in the design.

Subject: Re: SPAM-MED: [Bldg-sim] LEED NC: Existing or New Building

Yes, I agree that i should use the existing envelope for the existing conditions of the existing building and the ASHRAE Min for the new building.  But it comes down to whether EA Credit 1 follows the new building % or the existing building %.  it is a 2 point difference that could effect strategies for saving energy later in the design.

Rob

On Fri, Jul 8, 2011 at 10:44 AM, Jorge Torres-Coto <jtorres-coto at mbo1.com<mailto:jtorres-coto at mbo1.com>> wrote:
The baselines for the existing portion are what is there now.

The baselines for the new portion (addition) are ASHRAE definitions.

This should help considerably in your overall energy efficiency gains.

It all comes down to % new vs. % existing, on how much you can gain.

Steven Winter Associates Inc. (SWA) is seeking a Building Systems Engineer SWA's Connecticut Office -

Building Systems Engineer



SWA is seeking an engineer to assist in improving performance of homes and residential systems through research, design, and field evaluations. The successful candidate will be an integral part of our expanding residential energy services group. The position will focus primarily on the building envelope and mechanical systems in homes, but IEQ, moisture management, reducing and reusing materials, and other aspects of sustainable home construction will also be relevant. This person will be fully responsible for the planning and execution of projects from the proposal stage through project completion.

Work will include design, research, modeling, performance testing, monitoring, and product development. The successful candidate will have a strong technical background with a solid, working knowledge of residential systems and building science. Familiarities with residential HVAC design (Manual J/D) and energy modeling (REM/Rate, EnergyGauge, DOE-2, etc.) is desired. It is also desirable if the applicant is a RESNET certified HERS rater and/or BPI certified building analyst. The successful candidate will have excellent written and verbal communications skills, as well as interpersonal skills.

Reply in confidence to Steven Winter Associates, Inc., 50 Washington St., Norwalk, CT 06854, hr301 at swinter.com<mailto:301 at swinter.com>

This position offers excellent growth potential and an attractive compensation package / Equal Opportunity Employer.

Steven Winter Associates Inc. (SWA) is seeking a Residential Energy Analyst in SWA's Connecticut Office

Residential Energy Analyst

Steven Winter Associates, Inc. (SWA) is seeking an enthusiastic and skilled Engineer/Architect to join our expanding Residential Energy Services Group based in Norwalk, CT.

The successful candidate will assist in improving performance of homes and residential systems through research, design and field evaluations. Job responsibilities include, but are not limited to energy modeling; data collection, analysis and reporting; creating and manipulating spreadsheets and databases; graphic design assistance, reviewing standards and codes; building systems research and outreach.

Candidate must possess a strong technical background in building science, energy modeling and a working knowledge in residential systems. Hands on experience with energy modeling software such as REM/Rate, EnergyGauge, DOE-2 etc. is required. Must be proficient with Microsoft Office Suite (Word, Excel, and PowerPoint). Excellent written and verbal communication skills are necessary. Familiarity with design/drafting software such as AutoCAD, Google SketchUp, and Adobe InDesign is highly desirable.

Interested candidates can email their resume and portfolio (if available) in confidence to Human Resources, Steven Winter Associates, Inc., 50 Washington Street, Norwalk, CT, hr301 at swinter.com<mailto:hr301 at swinter.com>.

This position offers excellent growth potential and an attractive compensation package / Equal Opportunity Employer.

Yes, I agree that i should use the existing envelope for the existing
conditions of the existing building and the ASHRAE Min for the new
building.  But it comes down to whether EA Credit 1 follows the new
building % or the existing building %.  it is a 2 point difference that
could effect strategies for saving energy later in the design.

 

Rob

It all comes down to % new vs. % existing, on how much you can gain.

From ppeixeiro at lnec.pt  Tue Jul 12 02:03:27 2011
From: ppeixeiro at lnec.pt (Pedro Peixeiro)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 10:03:27 +0100
Subject: [Bldg-sim] insulation film on windows in EnergyPlus
In-Reply-To: <SNT113-W148ED8D1E8ECFBA85C6B48C6450@phx.gbl>
References: <SNT113-W148ED8D1E8ECFBA85C6B48C6450@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <4E1C0DDF.6070000@lnec.pt>

Jeong,

Shading coefficient is an older term that has been gradually substituted 
by the SHGC value. The object WindowMaterial:SimpleGlazingSystem allows 
you to enter this value directly. A quick search around the web shows 
that the SC should be around 1.15 * SHGC. But you should get some more 
reliable information on this. Also, it is recommended that you use the 
detailed input for windows and not the simple glazing system. If you 
can, get a hold of the solar transmittance and conductivity of your 
window and you should be able to use the detailed input.

Pedro.

From jones.0bj3 at gmail.com  Tue Jul 12 02:04:59 2011
From: jones.0bj3 at gmail.com (Marcus)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 11:04:59 +0200
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Energy Modeling for Complex Facade
In-Reply-To: <4E1ABD55.8020900@gmx.net>
References: <mailman.0.1310243442.15033.bldg-sim-onebuilding.org@lists.onebuilding.org>
	<4E1ABD55.8020900@gmx.net>
Message-ID: <CAEzj1z5+MBkdf2LY+UW9nu7e6-YN8oAnzH0AkDT6YjO692TqwA@mail.gmail.com>

I've had a little experience in complex envelope geometry, although I've
never used ArchiCAD or Ecodesigner. Generally speaking though, energy
modeling software can't use curved geometry. Any curved surface has to be
approximated using planar surfaces. Modeling software need this planar
geometry in it's internal algorithms, especially to calculate the radiative
heat transfer and shading.

The approach I have used, for Energyplus, is to manually mesh a curved
surface using many smaller triangular surfaces. There are likely ways to
automate the process if you have very complex and large geometry.

Cheers,

Marcus
-- 
Marcus Jones, LEED AP, M.Sc.
*Freelance energy consultant*
*Vienna, Austria*

From jones.0bj3 at gmail.com  Tue Jul 12 02:06:12 2011
From: jones.0bj3 at gmail.com (Marcus)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 11:06:12 +0200
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Fwd:  insulation film on windows in EnergyPlus
In-Reply-To: <CAEzj1z6-64OuxQx=+2F-5TurKpjZp6pDLFgLXczJq9vSzw30Hw@mail.gmail.com>
References: <SNT113-W148ED8D1E8ECFBA85C6B48C6450@phx.gbl>
	<CAEzj1z6-64OuxQx=+2F-5TurKpjZp6pDLFgLXczJq9vSzw30Hw@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <CAEzj1z7Px5F5xQ18OzF2CjRYR9+Sw2=YhG1dPMMyj-VKqCRbUg@mail.gmail.com>

I don't think a WindowMaterial:Shade will properly account for the film, I
would instead look into creating your window in the software WINDOW from
LBL. This software can model detailed layer-by-layer windows with frames and
full spectral data, which can then be exported into EnergyPlus.

http://windows.lbl.gov/software/window/window.html

Good luck and I hope you let us know your solution!

P.S. EnergyPlus talks about the SHGC or g-Value of windows, not shading
coefficients. You can convert between them.

Marcus

Marcus Jones, LEED AP, M.Sc.
*Freelance energy consultant*
*Vienna, Austria*

From paul at zed-uk.com  Tue Jul 12 02:27:22 2011
From: paul at zed-uk.com (=?utf-8?B?UGF1bCBDYXJleQ==?=)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 10:27:22 +0100
Subject: [Bldg-sim]
	=?utf-8?q?Fwd=3A__insulation_film_on_windows_in_Energy?=
	=?utf-8?q?Plus?=
Message-ID: <20110712093157.EA1BB8DB01F0@zapata.dreamhost.com>

Jeong, Marcus, et al

That does of course assume you can get the properties of the window film from the manufacturer, which for new films can be easier said than done. The other issue is that many providers of the product ( not necessarily the manufacturers here) provide information for the film having been tested applied to a clear float glass and sometimes even for entire units, so it can be sometimes difficult to get the actual performance data of the film.

Shading coefficient is a quick way of including shades, etc in some other programs still use rather than the way EnergyPlus takes everything back to first principles calculations. Could still be useful if you are looking at say "living wall" features for example without having to go into the complexity of using a "shading" block or surface with a scheduled transmissivity.

Regards
Paul

	<CAEzj1z5+MBkdf2LY+UW9nu7e6-YN8oAnzH0AkDT6YjO692TqwA@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4E1C1598.2010800@gmx.net>

12.07.2011 11:04 Marcus wrote:
> I've had a little experience in complex envelope geometry, although I've
> never used ArchiCAD or Ecodesigner. Generally speaking though, energy
> modeling software can't use curved geometry. Any curved surface has to
> be approximated using planar surfaces. Modeling software need this
> planar geometry in it's internal algorithms, especially to calculate the
> radiative heat transfer and shading.
>
> The approach I have used, for Energyplus, is to manually mesh a curved
> surface using many smaller triangular surfaces. There are likely ways to
> automate the process if you have very complex and large geometry.

Hi!

Meshing geometry may be necessary, but will result to a complex model 
with a lot of potential for error. From what I understand, this may 
result in a huge number of polygons, as "spiral 3d shading" sounds a lot 
like smooth shapes possibly highly specular surfaces (which is why I was 
asking for details). Archicad can output meshes (I remember obj), but it 
would be important to check mesh quality.

It would be interesting to assess the need for such a model here, or 
whether the performance could be represented by a simple plane with 
transmissive properties according to the more complex shading. If 
surfaces are diffuse and view is blocked, maybe a flat, 
diffuse-transmissive material could replace the shading's complex 
geometry. If there is direct view through the shading, as long as the 
surfaces are not specular, the actual shape should not matter and a much 
simplified replacement according to thickness and angles related to the 
facade normal would probably be sufficient. If it is a curvy specular 
shading, it is going to be difficult!

Cheers, Lars.

From Vikram.Sami at perkinswill.com  Tue Jul 12 08:10:43 2011
From: Vikram.Sami at perkinswill.com (Sami, Vikram)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 10:10:43 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Energy Modeling for Complex Facade
In-Reply-To: <4E1C1598.2010800@gmx.net>
References: <mailman.0.1310243442.15033.bldg-sim-onebuilding.org@lists.onebuilding.org>
	<4E1ABD55.8020900@gmx.net>
	<CAEzj1z5+MBkdf2LY+UW9nu7e6-YN8oAnzH0AkDT6YjO692TqwA@mail.gmail.com>
	<4E1C1598.2010800@gmx.net>
Message-ID: <5CFB01489F62D143B61289AF32DC98B104291C77E5@chi01exmb.perkinswill.net>

I agree with Lars here. Energy efficiency applies to the modeling process too.

You might want to start out with a sensitivity study just to establish what percentage of the total project energy, cooling & heating loads are coming from the fa?ade. If its relatively small, then it doesn't make sense to use a ton of resources modeling it exactly, and you might want to create an approximate equivalent - either by simplifying the geometry or by scheduling transmittance.

If it is actually a large percentage of your building load, then I would look a little bit more in depth as to how it might be modeled.

The CaGBC allows using a pro-rated point scale based on the relative floor areas of new and existing.  Not sure if this is the same for USGBC.

Kyle

From ncaton at smithboucher.com  Tue Jul 12 09:17:17 2011
From: ncaton at smithboucher.com (Nick Caton)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 11:17:17 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Energy Modeling for Complex Facade
In-Reply-To: <5CFB01489F62D143B61289AF32DC98B104291C77E5@chi01exmb.perkinswill.net>
References: <mailman.0.1310243442.15033.bldg-sim-onebuilding.org@lists.onebuilding.org><4E1ABD55.8020900@gmx.net><CAEzj1z5+MBkdf2LY+UW9nu7e6-YN8oAnzH0AkDT6YjO692TqwA@mail.gmail.com><4E1C1598.2010800@gmx.net>
	<5CFB01489F62D143B61289AF32DC98B104291C77E5@chi01exmb.perkinswill.net>
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F057@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

Echoing others' advice using Einstein:  "A clever person solves a problem.  A wise person avoids it."  

 

A thought for "wisdom":  if you want to simplify the shading effect of an even helix, one might consider a helix looks identical from any angle along a horizontal plane - you might get a 'good enough' approximation at low sun angles using a simple 2D silhouette of the shape!

 

If you want to go the "accurate" route (now I'm trying to be clever... oh dear), I was intrigued by the challenge and after playing with the idea a bit in excel I can point you towards the math required to generate vertices for a helical shape... I think it's ultimately possible in eQuest, but it would be very hard.  Nothing I'd encourage/advocate in any sense... just sharing what I found for the curious.  I would much sooner seek a "reasonable approximation."

 

x(t) = r*cos (B*t)

y(t) = r*sin (B*t)

z(t) = t

 

where:

  coil height = period = 2pi/B  <-- set B = 2pi/(coil height)

  r = radius

  t = interval for points = angle between points, viewed from above (expressed in fractions of pi)

 

Those three functions along with the variables as described will return a helix in cartesian (xyz coordinates).  You could in theory generate a full set, then "offset" the original along the z-axis by the thickness of your actual "coil" to get the other half of the vertices.  Alternating between these sets, you could define a series of 2d planes which together would look pretty much like a coil spiraling as high as you'd want it to...  But you would undoubtedly save time by defining polygons for a height equal to a certain even number of periods, then copy/pasting the result as it would repeat for the resulting height.  I would definitely advise caution/consideration before setting the 't' variable to anything less than pi/6, lest you end up with magnitudes more polygons beyond reason. 

 

For your sake, I'm starting to hope you don't even read past the first few lines... haha!

 

~Nick

 

PS: Here's a screengrab of what I came up with for illustration:

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org [mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Sami, Vikram
Sent: Tuesday, July 12, 2011 10:11 AM
To: Lars O. Grobe; bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Energy Modeling for Complex Facade

 

I agree with Lars here. Energy efficiency applies to the modeling process too.

 

You might want to start out with a sensitivity study just to establish what percentage of the total project energy, cooling & heating loads are coming from the fa?ade. If its relatively small, then it doesn't make sense to use a ton of resources modeling it exactly, and you might want to create an approximate equivalent - either by simplifying the geometry or by scheduling transmittance.

 

If it is actually a large percentage of your building load, then I would look a little bit more in depth as to how it might be modeled.

 

 

 

 

Vikram Sami, LEED AP BD+C

Sustainable Design Analyst

1315 Peachtree St. NE, Atlanta, GA 30309

t: 404-443-7462    f: 404.892.5823       e: vikram.sami at perkinswill.com   www.perkinswill.com

Perkins+Will.  Ideas + buildings that honor the broader goals of society

 

 

-----Original Message-----

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org [mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Lars O. Grobe

Sent: Tuesday, July 12, 2011 5:36 AM

To: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org

Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Energy Modeling for Complex Facade

 

12.07.2011 11:04 Marcus wrote:

> I've had a little experience in complex envelope geometry, although I've

> never used ArchiCAD or Ecodesigner. Generally speaking though, energy

> modeling software can't use curved geometry. Any curved surface has to

> be approximated using planar surfaces. Modeling software need this

> planar geometry in it's internal algorithms, especially to calculate the

> radiative heat transfer and shading.

> 

> The approach I have used, for Energyplus, is to manually mesh a curved

> surface using many smaller triangular surfaces. There are likely ways to

> automate the process if you have very complex and large geometry.

 

Hi!

 

Meshing geometry may be necessary, but will result to a complex model

with a lot of potential for error. From what I understand, this may

result in a huge number of polygons, as "spiral 3d shading" sounds a lot

like smooth shapes possibly highly specular surfaces (which is why I was

asking for details). Archicad can output meshes (I remember obj), but it

would be important to check mesh quality.

 

It would be interesting to assess the need for such a model here, or

whether the performance could be represented by a simple plane with

transmissive properties according to the more complex shading. If

surfaces are diffuse and view is blocked, maybe a flat,

diffuse-transmissive material could replace the shading's complex

geometry. If there is direct view through the shading, as long as the

surfaces are not specular, the actual shape should not matter and a much

simplified replacement according to thickness and angles related to the

facade normal would probably be sufficient. If it is a curvy specular

shading, it is going to be difficult!

 

Cheers, Lars.

that has more influence ( if room has South and West walls, I attach to a

that LEED asks to submit a plan with properly modeled zones which have to
have their own control.
The question is

can model as one zone with one control.

From cmg750 at gmail.com  Tue Jul 12 18:29:16 2011
From: cmg750 at gmail.com (Carol Gardner)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2011 18:29:16 -0700

In-Reply-To: <1310498356.6349.YahooMailNeo@web126106.mail.ne1.yahoo.com>

	<1310498356.6349.YahooMailNeo@web126106.mail.ne1.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <CA+Jz3pcgnNqY8+rQdKgg1+aUGUu5_+KyYTxHFW5fdZvAoCih0Q@mail.gmail.com>

Evgenia,

The eQUEST Tutorial has a good discussion of zoning in it. Std 90 requires a
perimeter/core zoning pattern at least.

John is correct to point you to mechanical system zones, but there are times
that you won't need to put each and every zone in. An example of one of them
is a multi-family building with a number of units on each exposure. They can
be combined into a thermal block, as discussed in Table G3.1 of Standard 90.
When you use this type of zoning each corner unit is typically handled as a
separate zone since they have 2 exposures.

I hope that answers your question. Zoning is very important and can make or
break your model.

Best,

Carol

From robbyoylear at gmail.com  Wed Jul 13 09:36:14 2011
From: robbyoylear at gmail.com (Robby Oylear)
Date: Wed, 13 Jul 2011 09:36:14 -0700
Subject: [Bldg-sim] SHGC adjustment and LEED impact q?
In-Reply-To: <E52D1AEF5C5D364281BD7ADE1FDC0CE0976346780B@mamail.mazzetti.com>
References: <E52D1AEF5C5D364281BD7ADE1FDC0CE0976346780B@mamail.mazzetti.com>
Message-ID: <CAMXhxQUmv8d4n35PXP5UrhKneLHzQyspybLZKJDsPZkNEsLnrA@mail.gmail.com>

Hi Nikola,

In order to meet LEED EAp2 Minimum Energy Performance, the project must
comply with all mandatory provisions of ASHRAE 90.1-2007.  Section 5.4.2
Fenestration and Doors requires that fenestration performanc be determined
per section 5.8.2.  Section 5.8.2.5 Solar Heat Gain Coefficient requires
that the SHGC for the overall fenestration area (including framing) be
determined in accordance with NFRC 200.

If you read the exceptions, what you have done previously (use COG SHGC) is
an acceptable method per 5.8.2.5 exception b.  The method described in your
quote below is not acceptable unless you are provided with an NFRC
simulation or certified product number showing that the SHGC is 0.25 when
framing is accounted for per NFRC 200.

*Robby Oylear, **LEED?AP BD+C***

*Mechanical Project Engineer*

*Energy Analyst*



*RUSHING* | *D* 206-788-4571 | *C* 206-354-2721

*www.rushingco.com* <http://www.rushingco.com/>

From juran at summerconsultants.com  Thu Jul 14 05:24:35 2011
From: juran at summerconsultants.com (Anne Juran)

Subject: [Bldg-sim] SHGC adjustment and LEED impact q?
In-Reply-To: <CAMXhxQUmv8d4n35PXP5UrhKneLHzQyspybLZKJDsPZkNEsLnrA@mail.gmail.com>
References: <E52D1AEF5C5D364281BD7ADE1FDC0CE0976346780B@mamail.mazzetti.com>
	<CAMXhxQUmv8d4n35PXP5UrhKneLHzQyspybLZKJDsPZkNEsLnrA@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <005d01cc4220$fe8de300$fba9a900$@com>

Yes, lots of framing results in a system SHGC lower than the COG SHGC.  I
know Kawneer does publish their data, although it is per AAMA 507, which is
a testing standard similar to NFRC 200, which is what 90.1 requires. See
page 34 of
http://www.kawneer.com/kawneer/north_america/catalog/pdf/1600_Wall_Sys1__E--
A.pdf for an example.

 

I have submitted a LEED project using the manufacturer's info, but it is
currently under review, so I cannot give you a good answer.  I would hope
that as long as you have the manufacturer's published info per NFRC 200 you
would be okay.

 

 

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org
[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Robby Oylear
Sent: Wednesday, July 13, 2011 12:36 PM
To: Nikola Kravik
Cc: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] SHGC adjustment and LEED impact q?

 

Hi Nikola,

 

In order to meet LEED EAp2 Minimum Energy Performance, the project must
comply with all mandatory provisions of ASHRAE 90.1-2007.  Section 5.4.2
Fenestration and Doors requires that fenestration performanc be determined
per section 5.8.2.  Section 5.8.2.5 Solar Heat Gain Coefficient requires
that the SHGC for the overall fenestration area (including framing) be
determined in accordance with NFRC 200.  

 

If you read the exceptions, what you have done previously (use COG SHGC) is
an acceptable method per 5.8.2.5 exception b.  The method described in your
quote below is not acceptable unless you are provided with an NFRC
simulation or certified product number showing that the SHGC is 0.25 when
framing is accounted for per NFRC 200.

 

Robby Oylear, LEEDRAP BD+C

Mechanical Project Engineer

Energy Analyst

 

RUSHING | D 206-788-4571 | C 206-354-2721

 <http://www.rushingco.com/> www.rushingco.com 

 

On Tue, Jul 12, 2011 at 1:06 PM, Nikola Kravik <nkravik at mazzetti.com> wrote:

Hi,

 

Can the statement on the SHGC reduction below (0.29 down to 0.25) be true if
you have a lot of framing? 

I've always increased the U value to adjust for the frame effect, but never
decreased the SHGC~ 13%; I've actually left it same as the COG SHGC, now I'm
asked to plug this 0.25 instead of 0.29;

Can anyone share thoughts if this will fly with LEED v3 since I believe that
a 4'x6' window can't be slightly lower on SHGC but not 13%?

 

...that
<http://www.viracon.com/downloads/resources/BDESSustainableFlyer.pdf> the
glazing selected (VNE1-63) has the highest light-to-solar-gain ratio of any
one available.  The SHGC at center-of-glass is 0.29, and scaled to account
for framing the unit SHGC is 0.25-exactly the ASHRAE reference value for our
climate zone 2A..referring to ASH 90.1-2007;

 

 

 

I appreciate your feedback.

 

Cheers,

n

From oconnor_dj at hotmail.com  Thu Jul 14 14:13:52 2011

Subject: [Bldg-sim] Energy Model Costs

Hey,
 
I know there was a discussion on this a few months back but one 
area which the discussion did not cover was regarding the time it takes 
for energy model simulations. I am currently working on my first LEED 
project which is a complex university lab building. I am using IES VE to
 model the building. After a long process I am finally nearing project 
completion. However, I found myself spending a huge amount of time 
waiting during simulations. Using a computer with the spec recommended 
by IES VE for large projects, the full PRM simulation took approximately
 30 hours (6 hours for each - proposed, baseline 0, baseline 90, 
baseline 180 and baseline 270). As my bosses are looking to me for ideas
 regarding cost for future projects, does one account for simulation 
time in the price or how is this done?
 
Any help is greatly appreciated!
 
Kind Regards,
 
Donal

From dse at grummanbutkus.com  Thu Jul 14 14:26:57 2011
From: dse at grummanbutkus.com (David Eldridge)
Date: Thu, 14 Jul 2011 16:26:57 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Energy Model Costs
In-Reply-To: <DUB103-w499CA005D376DDB4EDB8C8E0460@phx.gbl>
References: <DUB103-w499CA005D376DDB4EDB8C8E0460@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <08831d8339d3346cffa8e2bac6dc83e6@mail.gmail.com>

Try to run models like that at night before you leave.



Most likely you?ll see some savings in run-time (whichever program you use)
by reducing the number of objects in your next project where you see the
opportunity to combine things in a reasonable manner.



I wouldn?t normally include any significant time allocation for simulation
time as you describe, but if you are going to incur it I guess you better
let your boss know to account for that.  Hopefully you can find something
else to work on ahead of time while the calculation proceeds if your next
project has similarly long run times.



David

From paul at zed-uk.com  Thu Jul 14 15:12:00 2011
From: paul at zed-uk.com (=?utf-8?B?UGF1bCBDYXJleQ==?=)
Date: Thu, 14 Jul 2011 23:12:00 +0100
Subject: [Bldg-sim] =?utf-8?q?Energy_Model_Costs?=
Message-ID: <20110714221650.9DB31984007@zapata.dreamhost.com>

Donal

Set it up during the day and leave it running over night and have a minimum of 2 machines, so when its tied up running simulations you can still get on with other stuff.

Depending on number of zones that sounds pretty reasonable to me. I've had energyplus models I got bored with letting them run after 4 days. Ok its quicker now, but the Apache bit of IES is always a bit quicker than most. The issue comes when you start to take account of the Outcast and Radiance calculations for daylight linking control, all of which energyplus does at the same time as running simulations.

You should obviously take the time into account if you are setting up model, etc but I'd be very surprised if you can get away with charging for computational time. CFD people can get away with that as they are tying up processors for long periods often right across a network and it often does take longer.

Just my own opinion of course, but I'd be surprised if anyone on this list gets away with that. If you do, please don't tell me, just well done to you...;-)

Regards
Paul

From jim at buildingperformanceteam.com  Thu Jul 14 15:51:53 2011
From: jim at buildingperformanceteam.com (Jim Dirkes)

Message-ID: <00ee01cc4278$a1469d10$e3d3d730$@buildingperformanceteam.com>

My two cents?..

If it takes 6 hours, you?ve probably got too much detail in the model, e.g. too many zones, too many surfaces, shading elements that do little, too many individual windows when they could be combined, etc.

There are exceptions, of course, , but I think there are few models which NEED a very high level of detail.

You might ask one of the IES support folk their opinion of your model and for recommendations to make it run faster.

 

The Building Performance Team

I second James. Also one place where run time does really affect you
is in trouble shooting a model. If you are running new system types or
a complex control and need to do a number of test runs to get
everything operating properly, having a simulation program that takes
lots of time can be difficult (even when running only a partial year
simulation)

Karen

Subject: [Bldg-sim] TC Chan Center to Host UN Symposium
Message-ID: <001201cc4313$ebc0ea30$c342be90$@upenn.edu>

TC Chan Center to Host UN Symposium

 

On October 27th and 28th, 2011, the T.C. Chan Center will be hosting the
United Nations Environmental Program - Sustainable Buildings Climate
Initiative (UNEP-SBCI) Symposia at the University of Pennsylvania. This
event will bring together notable speakers from around the world to share
knowledge on current events, technology, and policy to promote sustainable
building practices on a regional and global scale. 

 

This high-profile symposium is a yearly event held by the UNEP-SBCI to bring
together business, academics and non-profit organizations involved with
sustainable building practices. Industry and technology presentations and
panel discussions will be held along with governmental and policy
contributions from local, national and international perspectives. The
symposium includes two days of presentations, speakers and panels, and a
networking reception. 

 

For more information about the symposia or to express interest in attending
the event, please contact the T.C. Chan Center at TCChan at design.upenn.edu.

 

Sincerely, 

 

Dr. Ali Malkawi

Founder and Director, T.C. Chan Center for Building Simulation and Energy
Studies

Professor, School of Design, University of Pennsylvania 

www.tcchancenter.com

From ncaton at smithboucher.com  Mon Jul 18 09:36:42 2011
From: ncaton at smithboucher.com (Nick Caton)
Date: Mon, 18 Jul 2011 11:36:42 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Suggestions for model reviews
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F3D2@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

Recently, I've had an odd string of personal contacts and strangers
alike confiding with me and demonstrating their frustrations over energy
model reviews.  As an outside-party, it's actually largely entertaining
to hear, and puts a few things in perspective.  We are all human, and we
all share common emotions when put under the spotlight, but how we put
those feelings to action makes all the difference between an
easy/productive review process and a train wreck...

 

I've had the pleasure of treading both paths, and I thought it might be
helpful for modelers of all skill/experience levels to share a few tips
I've developed to manage the stress-level in your energy model reviews.
A few reviewers out there might also benefit to turn this advice around
and apply it to their practices as well:

 

1.       Do not assume your reviewer knows less than you.  

a.       Recognize that stuff gets lost in translation - energy modeling
minutiae are rarely easy to communicate in text format.

b.      It's human to feel annoyed when your work is questioned, but
practice patience.  Odds are you and/or your reviewer are going to learn
something as a result. 

c.       If you ever catch yourself thinking/responding in a dismissive
or condescending fashion, stop.  Your heart is in the wrong place
(however right you might be) and you're missing the point of an
objective review.  Take a breather!

2.       Do not assume your reviewer knows more than you, either.  

a.       Do not be afraid to challenge or question a query, but prepare
and present a solid case for any contrary position.  

b.      It is very easy to run yourself in circles trying to
explain/justify yourself when it isn't really necessary.  

c.       Reviewers can and should make it plain when an in-depth
explanation is required.

3.       Be prepared to change your mind!  For better or for worse,
reviews are ultimately beneficial to all parties involved, because they
provide an excellent opportunity to learn a thing or two.  Allow your
personal expertise to continually evolve and incorporate, or at least
recognize, others' perspectives and practices.  They may serve you well
personally in the future!

4.       Once in a while, you may have to work with an individual who is
truly stubborn, unreasonable and is not willing to participate in any
objective discussion.  If a review should fail to "close out" over such
behavior, the best you can hope for is to document communications so you
can later make apparent in hindsight that you were proactive, and
pulling your weight to move the process forward.  

 

If anyone has similar guiding principles or tips to share (from either
side of the reviewing fence), I think it would be much appreciated =)!

 

~Nick

 

 

 

NICK CATON, P.E.

SENIOR ENGINEER

 

Smith & Boucher Engineers

25501 west valley parkway, suite 200

olathe, ks 66061

direct 913.344.0036

fax 913.345.0617

www.smithboucher.com

From nathanm at rushingco.com  Mon Jul 18 10:04:04 2011
From: nathanm at rushingco.com (Nathan Miller)
Date: Mon, 18 Jul 2011 10:04:04 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Suggestions for model reviews
In-Reply-To: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F3D2@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
References: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F3D2@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
Message-ID: <017601cc456c$b28ab100$17a01300$@com>

Regarding item #4 (stubborn, unreasonable reviewer), when I am responding
to an item that I absolutely do not agree with the reviewer's
interpretation, I usually take the following approach:

 

1)      Lay out my full argument as respectfully as possible. Usually this
involves screen shots of the specific code and/or ASHRAE 90.1 sections
that I believe bolsters my argument. 

2)      Present the results that I believe accurately reflect the
requirements of the modeling protocol.

3)      Put in a qualifying statement along the lines of "However, if the
reviewer does not believe this is the correct interpretation of this
ASHRAE 90.1 requirement."

4)      Present the results that reflect the approach that the reviewer
suggested or was clearly implying. 

 

This way the ball is in their court to make the final call, and they have
the full information necessary to decide how many "points" (assuming LEED
compliance is the goal) to award for the project. They don't have to try
to approximate how much savings is realistic if they disagree with my
interpretation, and my client has the most flexibility in deciding if they
want to accept how the reviewer has ruled, or if it is worth trying to
appeal. 

 

It doesn't do any good to paint yourself into a corner where the reviewer
might still disagree with your modeling methodology, but you have already
used your one shot at responding to comments. 

 

Nathan Miller, PE, LEEDRAP

Senior Energy Engineer/Mechanical Engineer

 

D 206-788-4577

 <http://www.rushingco.com/> www.rushingco.com

Excellent comments!

Nick's point #1 (reviewer may be smarter than you) is one that is
particularly hard for many engineers to swallow.  I won't tell you how many
times I've started a meeting or discussion thinking I was the "brightest
penny in the bucket", only to find out that others had much better insights
than I.  I'm more humble now than 30 years ago.

Each of us travels somewhat similar paths if we're in the same industry, but
it's a very large industry and our reviewer almost certainly has not had the
same experiences.  Give him (her) the same patience that we'd like to
receive ourselves!

My problem isn't generally with the reviewer, it's with the LEED review
process.  Not being able to call the reviewers up and discuss comments
over the phone is a ridiculous waste of everyone's time.  Additionally, a
real review would involve looking at the model, not just a random
selection of I/O.  

 

I usually propose that clients go "beyond LEED" at this point, to save a
considerable amount of money that can be used to pay for additional EEMs
and because the documentation and process is so onerous.

a.       Recognize that stuff gets lost in translation - energy modeling
minutiae are rarely easy to communicate in text format.

b.      It's human to feel annoyed when your work is questioned, but
practice patience.  Odds are you and/or your reviewer are going to learn
something as a result. 

c.       If you ever catch yourself thinking/responding in a dismissive or
condescending fashion, stop.  Your heart is in the wrong place (however
right you might be) and you're missing the point of an objective review.
Take a breather!

2.       Do not assume your reviewer knows more than you, either.  

a.       Do not be afraid to challenge or question a query, but prepare
and present a solid case for any contrary position.  

b.      It is very easy to run yourself in circles trying to
explain/justify yourself when it isn't really necessary.  

c.       Reviewers can and should make it plain when an in-depth
explanation is required.

3.       Be prepared to change your mind!  For better or for worse,
reviews are ultimately beneficial to all parties involved, because they
provide an excellent opportunity to learn a thing or two.  Allow your
personal expertise to continually evolve and incorporate, or at least
recognize, others' perspectives and practices.  They may serve you well
personally in the future!

4.       Once in a while, you may have to work with an individual who is
truly stubborn, unreasonable and is not willing to participate in any
objective discussion.  If a review should fail to "close out" over such
behavior, the best you can hope for is to document communications so you
can later make apparent in hindsight that you were proactive, and pulling
your weight to move the process forward.  

 

If anyone has similar guiding principles or tips to share (from either
side of the reviewing fence), I think it would be much appreciated =)!

 

~Nick

 

cid:489575314 at 22072009-0ABB

I think this is how to do it: If you know the what the film is, I think you can create the new glass+film combo in LBNL's OPTICS. You can then import that new glass/film into LBNL's WINDOW. You should finally be able to make a DOE report file in WINDOW and import in eQUEST.

From ncaton at smithboucher.com  Tue Jul 19 13:59:01 2011
From: ncaton at smithboucher.com (Nick Caton)
Date: Tue, 19 Jul 2011 15:59:01 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached...
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F52E@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

I have a precedent question for everyone... this is not a "how to"
procedural question for any particular software, but to simply feel out
what has worked for others in the past:

 

Here's a quick description of the situation at-hand:  a VAV terminal
unit with a "normal" minimum damper position (say, 30%) is tied by relay
to the space occupancy sensor, which also controls the lights.  Upon
sensing space vacancy, the minimum airflow damper position is reset to
0% (airflow is permitted to stop, provided thermostat temperature set
point is satisfied).  Upon sensing occupancy, the preset minimum damper
position is restored.

 

For LEED/USGBC-reviewed energy models, can anyone relate success in
modeling this or a similar energy-saving behavior in the proposed model,
distinct from the baseline?  If so, what approach did you use to model
this behavior and/or quantify the energy savings? 

 

I was discussing the possibilities with some colleagues and have a few
ideas for approaches that might all be justifiable, ranging from simple
(and quick) to complex (and time-consuming).  

 

There are likely multiple "right" answers here, but I am hoping to
identify some precedent to understand what we can anticipate the LEED
reviewership will accept.  

 

Thanks in advance!

 

~Nick

 

 

 

NICK CATON, P.E.

SENIOR ENGINEER

olathe, ks 66061

direct 913.344.0036

All,

The Trane Trace energy cost budget report shows no cooling energy, despite having a cooling plant modeled.  Also, the yearly energy cost reported is zero, despite having a reported yearly electricity usage.  Is there a bug in the program?.  Has anyone experience this?  

A little background on the plants being modeled.  The cooling plant is modeled as 10 water source heat pumps piped in parallel along with a district cooling plant under the cooling plants tab.  The primary purpose of the heat pumps is to provide heating while the district cooling plant provides cooling to the building.  The heat pumps take chilled water return from the campus loop and reject the heat to the building, while returning chilled water back to the campus loop.

From John.S.Eurek at usace.army.mil  Wed Jul 20 06:30:56 2011
From: John.S.Eurek at usace.army.mil (Eurek, John S NWO)
Date: Wed, 20 Jul 2011 08:30:56 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached... (UNCLASSIFIED)

References: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F52E@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
Message-ID: <57E9627AE82514439A8F543A61BFDD68087B3396@NWO-ML3OMA.nwo.ds.usace.army.mil>

Classification: UNCLASSIFIED
Caveats: NONE

Nick,

I would do it with schedules.  This would be the long method.

I have the same controls and have not got around to model it.  I would also
make many schedules.  A different schedule for occupancy, lights, equipment,
ect.  Also if this applies to a lot of rooms, you'd likely make multiple
schedules, one assuming that the room is empty from 9~10 another room empty
from 2~3.  (The inverse for meeting rooms, if you have multiple meeting
rooms, not all meetings will happen at the same time.)


"Is Freedom a small price to pay to stop Global Warming?"

John Eurek PE, LEED AP
Mechanical Engineer,

From JHANSEN at ghtltd.com  Wed Jul 20 06:40:12 2011
From: JHANSEN at ghtltd.com (James Hansen)
Date: Wed, 20 Jul 2011 09:40:12 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached... (UNCLASSIFIED)
In-Reply-To: <57E9627AE82514439A8F543A61BFDD68087B3396@NWO-ML3OMA.nwo.ds.usace.army.mil>
References: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F52E@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
	<57E9627AE82514439A8F543A61BFDD68087B3396@NWO-ML3OMA.nwo.ds.usace.army.mil>
Message-ID: <4A015401026CF44ABCC4D2A6A467AA1801752DB8@EXCHANGE1.cadd_group.ghtltd.com>

I agree with John, I've done this on a model before and its painful.  Assuming there are 10 hours in a work day, I set up 10 different occupancy and lighting schedules, each schedule being essentially identical except for a different hour of zero occupancy / lighting.  I then applied these 10 schedules to the different office zones on a floor so that specific areas were vacant from 8am-9am, some vacant from 9am-10am, etc.  And then for my system, I assumed relatively consistent 10% non-occupied conditions and reduced the OA at the system level accordingly.  If your non-occupied conditions are more drastic (30%), obviously you can set up your schedules differently.

However, if your primary air is also cooling (and not some sort of DOAS chilled water VAV box), then you are going to also have to specify 10 separate thermostat schedules (really 20 with heating and cooling) so that the rooms do not have unmet load hours.  I'm assuming you go into setback mode when the offices are unoccupied.

However, this was under v2.0, so that was a long time ago and reviewers were not as critical in their reviews.

GHT Limited
James Hansen, P.E., LEED AP
Senior Associate
1010 N. Glebe Road, Suite 200
Arlington, VA? 22201-4749

From: ncaton at smithboucher.com (Nick Caton)
Date: Wed, 20 Jul 2011 10:45:16 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached... (UNCLASSIFIED)
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F587@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

Thanks so much for the suggestions!

 

I myself have tread the "extensive, realistic fractional/thermostat
scheduling" path before under similar circumstances.  Upon facing a VERY
large project, where the amount of explicit scheduling required for that
approach is compounded by the sheer quantity and variety of occupied
spaces benefiting from this relay setup, I am challenged with
brainstorming any acceptable (by LEED reviewer) means of approximating
the same behavior/savings by simplifying the problem...

 

Here are some alternative ideas we've come up with so far  - I would
very much appreciate others' thoughts on these, or any further related
LEED/USGBC experiences to share as well:

1.       90.1 already prescribes how we quantify the savings of
occupancy sensors for installed lighting  (10 or 15% flat deduction on
the LPD).  Rather than define & justify the quantity and timing of
unoccupied hours between varying space types (murky waters at best), one
could instead reduce the loads incident on the affected systems by the
same percentage.  This might be accomplished by applying this 10/15%
deduction to the affected spaces' fractional load schedules (occupancy,
equipment & lighting).  One would need to tread carefully to avoid
"double-dipping" on any spaces already claiming a LPD deduction for
occupancy sensors.  Baseline model's schedules would remain unaffected
and would be documented alongside the modified ones to illustrate the
difference. 

2.       (Simpler to model, but requiring slightly more documentation):
Let's say a hospital has an annual average of 85% occupancy for all its
patient rooms.  Treating every other room normally, select a
representative sampling (considering envelope loads) of 15% of the
patient rooms.  Model those selected rooms as "empty" (set people,
lights and equipment loads = 0) but still conditioned to maintain the
thermostat setpoint (against loads incident from the envelope &
neighboring spaces). Apply the 0% minimum turndown behavior to those
"empty" rooms only.  Baseline model would receive identical treatments,
excepting the 0% turndown behavior.  Documentation would include
illustrating which zones were sampled against the others, and
justification for the net annual "occupancy rate" used for each space
type.

 

I have mixed feelings - obviously any simplification of the problem has
the potential to under/overstate the savings that might be found with a
more exhaustive scheduling approach, but may result in as good or even a
better estimation provided with solid documentation and execution.  Does
anyone think the above approaches could work well, or have any
suggestions to refine the strategies?  

 

Thanks again!

 

~Nick

 

 

 

 

NICK CATON, P.E.

SENIOR ENGINEER

-----Original Message-----
From: James Hansen [mailto:JHANSEN at ghtltd.com] 
Sent: Wednesday, July 20, 2011 8:40 AM
To: Eurek, John S NWO; Nick Caton; bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: RE: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached... (UNCLASSIFIED)

 

I agree with John, I've done this on a model before and its painful.
Assuming there are 10 hours in a work day, I set up 10 different
occupancy and lighting schedules, each schedule being essentially
identical except for a different hour of zero occupancy / lighting.  I
then applied these 10 schedules to the different office zones on a floor
so that specific areas were vacant from 8am-9am, some vacant from
9am-10am, etc.  And then for my system, I assumed relatively consistent
10% non-occupied conditions and reduced the OA at the system level
accordingly.  If your non-occupied conditions are more drastic (30%),
obviously you can set up your schedules differently.

 

However, if your primary air is also cooling (and not some sort of DOAS
chilled water VAV box), then you are going to also have to specify 10
separate thermostat schedules (really 20 with heating and cooling) so
that the rooms do not have unmet load hours.  I'm assuming you go into
setback mode when the offices are unoccupied.

 

However, this was under v2.0, so that was a long time ago and reviewers
were not as critical in their reviews.

Classification: UNCLASSIFIED

Nick,

 

I would do it with schedules.  This would be the long method.

 

I have the same controls and have not got around to model it.  I would
also

make many schedules.  A different schedule for occupancy, lights,
equipment,

ect.  Also if this applies to a lot of rooms, you'd likely make multiple

schedules, one assuming that the room is empty from 9~10 another room
empty

from 2~3.  (The inverse for meeting rooms, if you have multiple meeting

rooms, not all meetings will happen at the same time.)

 

 

"Is Freedom a small price to pay to stop Global Warming?"

-----Original Message-----

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org

[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Nick Caton

Sent: Tuesday, July 19, 2011 3:59 PM

To: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org

Subject: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached...

 

I have a precedent question for everyone... this is not a "how to"
procedural

question for any particular software, but to simply feel out what has
worked

for others in the past:

 

 

 

Here's a quick description of the situation at-hand:  a VAV terminal
unit

with a "normal" minimum damper position (say, 30%) is tied by relay to
the

space occupancy sensor, which also controls the lights.  Upon sensing
space

vacancy, the minimum airflow damper position is reset to 0% (airflow is

permitted to stop, provided thermostat temperature set point is
satisfied).

Upon sensing occupancy, the preset minimum damper position is restored.

 

 

 

For LEED/USGBC-reviewed energy models, can anyone relate success in
modeling

this or a similar energy-saving behavior in the proposed model, distinct
from

the baseline?  If so, what approach did you use to model this behavior
and/or

quantify the energy savings? 

 

 

 

I was discussing the possibilities with some colleagues and have a few
ideas

for approaches that might all be justifiable, ranging from simple (and
quick)

to complex (and time-consuming).  

 

 

 

There are likely multiple "right" answers here, but I am hoping to
identify

some precedent to understand what we can anticipate the LEED
reviewership

will accept.  

 

 

 

Thanks in advance!

 

 

 

~Nick

From smartin at shaunmartinconsulting.com  Wed Jul 20 09:20:25 2011
From: smartin at shaunmartinconsulting.com (Shaun Martin)
Date: Wed, 20 Jul 2011 09:20:25 -0700
Subject: [Bldg-sim] How have you approached... (UNCLASSIFIED)
In-Reply-To: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F587@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
References: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F587@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>
Message-ID: <95CFF13C50DA4F3DB6DEA291DA559173@SM>

Hi Nick,
 
To keep it simple, I would just derate the schedules, your 10/15% reduction
but on an hourly basis (ie one or more hours are moved to 0%/setback) and
apply the RESET-PRIORITY and MIN-RESET-FLOW keywords.  I think averaging it
out beforehand and explaining as part of your modelling methodology in your
report would take less time and be easier to explain.  I would start with a
small test model, to make sure you have the right percentages. 
 
Shaun

Thanks so much for the suggestions! 

 

I myself have tread the "extensive, realistic fractional/thermostat
scheduling" path before under similar circumstances.  Upon facing a VERY
large project, where the amount of explicit scheduling required for that
approach is compounded by the sheer quantity and variety of occupied spaces
benefiting from this relay setup, I am challenged with brainstorming any
acceptable (by LEED reviewer) means of approximating the same
behavior/savings by simplifying the problem.

 

Here are some alternative ideas we've come up with so far  - I would very
much appreciate others' thoughts on these, or any further related LEED/USGBC
experiences to share as well:

1.       90.1 already prescribes how we quantify the savings of occupancy
sensors for installed lighting  (10 or 15% flat deduction on the LPD).
Rather than define & justify the quantity and timing of unoccupied hours
between varying space types (murky waters at best), one could instead reduce
the loads incident on the affected systems by the same percentage.  This
might be accomplished by applying this 10/15% deduction to the affected
spaces' fractional load schedules (occupancy, equipment & lighting).  One
would need to tread carefully to avoid "double-dipping" on any spaces
already claiming a LPD deduction for occupancy sensors.  Baseline model's
schedules would remain unaffected and would be documented alongside the
modified ones to illustrate the difference. 

2.       (Simpler to model, but requiring slightly more documentation):
Let's say a hospital has an annual average of 85% occupancy for all its
patient rooms.  Treating every other room normally, select a representative
sampling (considering envelope loads) of 15% of the patient rooms.  Model
those selected rooms as "empty" (set people, lights and equipment loads = 0)
but still conditioned to maintain the thermostat setpoint (against loads
incident from the envelope & neighboring spaces). Apply the 0% minimum
turndown behavior to those "empty" rooms only.  Baseline model would receive
identical treatments, excepting the 0% turndown behavior.  Documentation
would include illustrating which zones were sampled against the others, and
justification for the net annual "occupancy rate" used for each space type.

 

I have mixed feelings - obviously any simplification of the problem has the
potential to under/overstate the savings that might be found with a more
exhaustive scheduling approach, but may result in as good or even a better
estimation provided with solid documentation and execution.  Does anyone
think the above approaches could work well, or have any suggestions to
refine the strategies?  

 

Thanks again!

 

~Nick

Message-ID: <CA+Jz3pdX41_1VddmCB5PkageK3qswccOAq6=Du9gjKJunFaDvw@mail.gmail.com>

I also agree with using schedules. I think the fact that Std 90 went from
"no schedule changes" to allowing it for DCV means they do too.

I think the best thing you can do to simplify your LEED review is to explain
to the reviewer what you are doing and why. As long as they can follow you,
it makes sense, and it is reasonably conservative, i.e. err on that side, it
should be acceptable to them.

Carol

From jglazer at gard.com  Wed Jul 20 12:24:01 2011
From: jglazer at gard.com (Jason Glazer)
Date: Wed, 20 Jul 2011 14:24:01 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Maximum allowed paramteric runs in EnergyPlus
In-Reply-To: <CAMDAJE8bN_XLEuZr6529oO0Z457MkDP5dssnVa3AMuDCK7sxGg@mail.gmail.com>
References: <CAMDAJE8bN_XLEuZr6529oO0Z457MkDP5dssnVa3AMuDCK7sxGg@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4E272B51.6040509@gard.com>

Yes you can do more than 100 parameterics in EnergyPlus. If 
you use just a text editor than just add more parametrics to 
the IDF file and it should work. If you use IDF Editor, you 
can add additional fields in the Energy+.IDD file for the 
parametric objects. Add as many fields as you need by 
copying the last field and changing the numbering as 
appropriate.

jason

From juran at summerconsultants.com  Wed Jul 20 13:39:27 2011
From: juran at summerconsultants.com (Anne Juran)
Date: Wed, 20 Jul 2011 16:39:27 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] No reported cooling energy Trane Trace 700
In-Reply-To: <D0AEAD83419A1848820FD3EDF46C99B501179B127B@AEI-MB.aeieng.lan>
References: <D0AEAD83419A1848820FD3EDF46C99B501179B127B@AEI-MB.aeieng.lan>
Message-ID: <044201cc471d$1eb71380$5c253a80$@com>

The simple answer would be to double check that your economics are entered
properly.  Also, did you have it scan for errors?

I have had files become corrupt with Trace and do crazy things like this
(that's why I archive at least daily and save all the old archives so I can
recover is something happens.)  Usually CDS can repair the file, so you may
just want to call them because they'll have more suggestions.  Also,
sometimes if you get an error right after it calculates weird things will
happen to the reports and you need to re-run the calculation.

You may also want to post to trace-users at lists.onebuilding.org and
http://energy-models.com/forum/energy-modeling-software/trace-700 - they're
ever-so-slightly more active for Trace questions.

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org
[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Thomas Vu
Sent: Tuesday, July 19, 2011 10:14 PM
To: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: [Bldg-sim] No reported cooling energy Trane Trace 700

All,

The Trane Trace energy cost budget report shows no cooling energy, despite
having a cooling plant modeled.  Also, the yearly energy cost reported is
zero, despite having a reported yearly electricity usage.  Is there a bug in
the program?.  Has anyone experience this?  

A little background on the plants being modeled.  The cooling plant is
modeled as 10 water source heat pumps piped in parallel along with a
district cooling plant under the cooling plants tab.  The primary purpose of
the heat pumps is to provide heating while the district cooling plant
provides cooling to the building.  The heat pumps take chilled water return
from the campus loop and reject the heat to the building, while returning
chilled water back to the campus loop.  

Thanks,

Thomas

I think this is how to do it: If you know the what the film is, I think you can create the new glass+film combo in LBNL's OPTICS. You can then import that new glass/film into LBNL's WINDOW. You should finally be able to make a DOE report file in WINDOW and import in eQUEST.

From michael.smith at wspfk.com  Thu Jul 21 07:18:38 2011
From: michael.smith at wspfk.com (Smith, Michael)
