Vinay

 

Simulating the VRV/VRF as a WSHP is effectively what the PVVT approach is modeling; it uses a heat pump as the heating source and can be set to water cooled. The PVVT allows for more complexity and control in how the water source heat pump operates. (i.e. variable speed compressor) See the attached presentation put together by Dana Troy and Mark Denyer, it?s quite helpful in their explanation.

Simulating the VRV/VRF as a WSHP is effectively what the PVVT approach is modeling; it uses a heat pump as the heating source and can be set to water cooled. Dear Akshay,

Did you mean 400-500 parameters or parametric jobs? Either way you can
try jeplus. It can handle half a million parametric jobs, which is only
limited by your computer's memory size. If you have a lot of parameters,
jeplus' project file and idf editor will help you keep track of them.

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org
[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Jason
Glazer
Sent: 20 July 2011 20:24
To: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Maximum allowed paramteric runs in EnergyPlus

Yes you can do more than 100 parameterics in EnergyPlus. If 
you use just a text editor than just add more parametrics to 
the IDF file and it should work. If you use IDF Editor, you 
can add additional fields in the Energy+.IDD file for the 
parametric objects. Add as many fields as you need by 
copying the last field and changing the numbering as 
appropriate.

jason

If have a hotel where the baseline system is PTAC, and those units are

Subject: [Bldg-sim] ventilation schedules for PTAC vs central outside

In-Reply-To: <4A015401026CF44ABCC4D2A6A467AA1801752F69@EXCHANGE1.cadd_group.ghtltd.com>
References: <4A015401026CF44ABCC4D2A6A467AA1801752F69@EXCHANGE1.cadd_group.ghtltd.com>
Message-ID: <CAMXhxQXU6k=mJzLPtZUNLv5w=uSXT-dUMrGaVzVw=HHVBOcX-g@mail.gmail.com>

As long as you're delivering the same quantity of outside air in both models
on an hourly basis, and accounting for how the proposed system will actually
operate, I'd say yes.  If for any reason your ventilation air operates
intermittently in the proposed design, you would need to schedule it the
same way in the baseline.

From jim at buildingperformanceteam.com  Thu Jul 21 10:49:06 2011
From: jim at buildingperformanceteam.com (Jim Dirkes)
Date: Thu, 21 Jul 2011 13:49:06 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] JWT Q2 - Energy Plus Calibration Experience
In-Reply-To: <EE3F700B3DD9D642BEA5D0B0A805C612204FB914D7@mail.hallam2000.com>
References: <EE3F700B3DD9D642BEA5D0B0A805C612204FB914D7@mail.hallam2000.com>
Message-ID: <008901cc47ce$7d9eb590$78dc20b0$@buildingperformanceteam.com>

Dear Josh,

As a start, "as-built" should really be "as-measured" by you or a reliable
tech.

The "measured" values should include:

.         Power for all major equipment

.         Flow for all major fans and pumps

.         Zone and discharge air temp setpoints

.         Schedules of (real) operation (If you have a BMS which trends
operation, that's a good place to start.  If not, plan to talk to
knowledgeable people)

.         Actual weather (you might check out www.weatheranalytics.com) 

.         There will still be unknowns / unknowables, such as infiltration.
Those can be adjusted within reasonable limits for a better fit.

.         Start by narrowing down the list of variables that are not know
with certainty and do NOT assume that things are as shown on plans!

 

p.s., I've worked up an interface between GenOpt (Dr. Michael Wetter at
LBNL) and E+ which makes iterative runs of E+ until it has a "Best fit".
It's still in the "development" stage, but seems to work well.  Please send
a private message if you're interested in trying it out.

 

The Building Performance Team
James V. Dirkes II, P.E., BEMP , LEED AP
1631 Acacia Drive NW
Grand Rapids, MI 49504
616 450 8653

From dse at grummanbutkus.com  Thu Jul 21 18:19:08 2011
From: dse at grummanbutkus.com (David Eldridge)
Date: Thu, 21 Jul 2011 20:19:08 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] JWT Q2 - Energy Plus Calibration Experience
In-Reply-To: <EE3F700B3DD9D642BEA5D0B0A805C612204FB914D7@mail.hallam2000.com>
References: <EE3F700B3DD9D642BEA5D0B0A805C612204FB914D7@mail.hallam2000.com>
Message-ID: <a8c22adbb794e4949331a9fdd5965212@mail.gmail.com>

What?s the level of calibration required?



I?d like to refer you to a paper by Dr. Z. O?Neil from the UTRC about
sensitivity analysis in an EnergyPlus model which might help you focus your
efforts on what to investigate.  This was given at the recent ASHRAE
conference in Montreal on Wednesday morning.



If the requested level of calibration is very high, then you?ll be looking
at a LOT of things?not just from deviations from model subroutine
accuracies, but due to occupancy and use related variances, as well as
developing your model of the control system.



I?d be very surprised if your main obstsacle is accuracy of a model
subroutine compared to ability of the users to provide data and the ability
to capture the actual control system logic.  (Or reflect user
changes/overrides, etc.)



David



*
*



David S. Eldridge, Jr., P.E., LEED AP BD+C, BEMP, BEAP, HBDP

*Grumman/Butkus Associates*

From John.S.Eurek at usace.army.mil  Fri Jul 22 13:30:05 2011
From: John.S.Eurek at usace.army.mil (Eurek, John S NWO)
Date: Fri, 22 Jul 2011 15:30:05 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt (UNCLASSIFIED)
In-Reply-To: <CA+Jz3pdX41_1VddmCB5PkageK3qswccOAq6=Du9gjKJunFaDvw@mail.gmail.com>
References: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F587@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com><95CFF13C50DA4F3DB6DEA291DA559173@SM>
	<CA+Jz3pdX41_1VddmCB5PkageK3qswccOAq6=Du9gjKJunFaDvw@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <57E9627AE82514439A8F543A61BFDD68087B3399@NWO-ML3OMA.nwo.ds.usace.army.mil>

Classification: UNCLASSIFIED
Caveats: NONE

Modelers.

One of my projects has server rooms which combined produce 143 MBH (more heat
than some home furnaces) and the servers run all the time.  
I am designing a ground source heat pump system for the building.  The energy
model shows that the geothermal well field is unbalanced by 522,500,000 btu.
I'm plan on using a snow melt system to get rid of the extra heat. (Snow melt
is a nice by product, the goal is to balance the well field's annual load.)

Has anyone designed this kind of system?
I'm not really sure how to model it?
I'm foggy on how to control it so I get rid of just the right amount of heat.
Has anyone designed this kind of system?

It is fun to get to design energy efficient systems and coming up with new
ideas, but it is hard to do research on them.  (I know snow melt isn't new,
geothermal isn't new, computer room cooling isn't new, but I haven't found
any articles tying the three together.)

From ncaton at smithboucher.com  Fri Jul 22 14:26:27 2011
From: ncaton at smithboucher.com (Nick Caton)
Date: Fri, 22 Jul 2011 16:26:27 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt (UNCLASSIFIED)
In-Reply-To: <57E9627AE82514439A8F543A61BFDD68087B3399@NWO-ML3OMA.nwo.ds.usace.army.mil>
References: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F587@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com><95CFF13C50DA4F3DB6DEA291DA559173@SM><CA+Jz3pdX41_1VddmCB5PkageK3qswccOAq6=Du9gjKJunFaDvw@mail.gmail.com>
	<57E9627AE82514439A8F543A61BFDD68087B3399@NWO-ML3OMA.nwo.ds.usace.army.mil>
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F7A6@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

I will say I got really excited this past year about the isolated idea
of combining snow melt with geothermal well field design...

For those unfamiliar, well fields in this part of the Midwest (KS/MO/NE)
historically heat up over the long haul, especially with intense
equipment loading like John's situation...  fields installed decades ago
commonly end up struggling and failing when the extreme summers roll
around, sooner or later. 

The most common pre-emptive/patch solution I've seen to tackle this
trend is to tack on (or set up infrastructure for) an exterior dry
cooler to reject excess heat from the loop. 

I pushed hard recently to consider a "snow melt" loop (even if not used
to functionally melt snow) as a better "free" means of keeping a
well-field in balance annually, but the real trick is in how to set it
up so that this extra loop it doesn't require active
maintenance/monitoring... Making a location for horizontal loops like
this is also a challenge in many cases where the land is not readily
available or has future expansions planned.  Most clients out there,
even those with motivated maintenance staffing, would really be best
served with a "fire and forget" system that could handle itself decades
down the road without requiring active monitoring/adjusting as the
seasons change and temps swing. Ultimately, I haven't yet come up with
any particularly great setup that I would consider fool-proof over the
span of decades, but I'd be very interested if others have
thoughts/details to share on how this might be achieved.

Conceivably, and to your specific questions John, I'd speculate the
actual controls would at a minimum need to define parameters
establishing a range of "target" loop temperatures for the cooling and
heating season (these would be based on 1st year loop simulations, to
start).  The "melt" loop routed near the surface (or embedded in
concrete where it might be safe), would be normally closed, but valves
would open for circulation when the outside temperatures* are conducive
to pushing the well field return temperatures in the desired direction.
Done cleverly, you could push the system to permit water below the
setpoint during the cooling season, and above the setpoint during the
heating season as well (this behavior would potentially occur in the
swing seasons - spring/fall).  I'm thinking the ideal controls would
constantly monitor and take advantage of free heat rejection & free heat
gains whenever applicable.

* Another point to consider:  What temperatures are ideal to monitor for
such a loop?  Outside air data may be easily available, but considering
the lag in topsoil temps and depending on what you choose for the actual
loop installation location you might do better to locate a sensor
elsewhere... A loop underneath black asphalt getting will experience
different temperatures than the same loop embedded in light-colored
hardscape or simply under exposed topsoil.

Northern climates might find the same setup of interest to primarily
gain free heat gain to keep loops in balance, routing the secondary loop
through building envelopes or similar massed structures that would pick
up extra heat in the summer months.

Suffice to say I find it an interesting topic - if anyone has ever come
across a design guide or white paper on this subject I'd also be very
interested to hear about it!

~Nick

Check the Oklahoma State Building and Environmental Thermal Systems
Research Group -- there are a number of papers on various topics related
to geothermal systems in hybrid, shallow, and other configurations.

http://www.hvac.okstate.edu/

There is also a great research project where snow melt was the primary
purpose of the geothermal system, which is not quite what you guys are
after but might offer some guidance.

David

	<CA+Jz3peUa1Bf2t+DywQXFnbyszb0ooyJ4rGVk-R22wdrKef+-Q@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <FCEBC0C28321F7479789B25A13030F6903E1F7B7@sandbinc2.sbi.smithboucher.com>

Hey Carol,

 

In a majority of 4-season climates (here in Kansas City too), I'd expect
you would not use such a secondary loop in the dead of summer where
night-time temperatures would not drop to practical/conducive levels for
heat rejection.

 

But to answer your question from other angles: this heat rejection/heat
gain loop would help in the summer months decades down the road, where
you've avoided overcooling/overheating the ground temperatures over
time.  

 

It would also help in that through late spring and early summer, you
would be able to cool through the workday/evening, then use cooler
nighttime/morning temperatures to offload the extra heat built up on a
daily basis, resulting in cooler net temperatures day-to-day.  This
would ultimately reduce and forestall the maximum temperatures that
would be reached during mid-to-late summer when you'd want this transfer
loop shut off 24/7.

 

I'll be digging into that OSU link David, thanks for the heads up!

 

~Nick

 

 

 

NICK CATON, P.E.

SENIOR ENGINEER

 

Smith & Boucher Engineers

25501 west valley parkway, suite 200

olathe, ks 66061

direct 913.344.0036

fax 913.345.0617

www.smithboucher.com

-----Original Message-----
From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org

[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Nick Caton
Sent: Friday, July 22, 2011 4:26 PM
To: Eurek, John S NWO; bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt (UNCLASSIFIED)


I will say I got really excited this past year about the isolated idea
of combining snow melt with geothermal well field design...

For those unfamiliar, well fields in this part of the Midwest (KS/MO/NE)
historically heat up over the long haul, especially with intense
equipment loading like John's situation...  fields installed decades ago
commonly end up struggling and failing when the extreme summers roll
around, sooner or later.

The most common pre-emptive/patch solution I've seen to tackle this
trend is to tack on (or set up infrastructure for) an exterior dry
cooler to reject excess heat from the loop.

I pushed hard recently to consider a "snow melt" loop (even if not used
to functionally melt snow) as a better "free" means of keeping a
well-field in balance annually, but the real trick is in how to set it
up so that this extra loop it doesn't require active
maintenance/monitoring... Making a location for horizontal loops like
this is also a challenge in many cases where the land is not readily
available or has future expansions planned.  Most clients out there,
even those with motivated maintenance staffing, would really be best
served with a "fire and forget" system that could handle itself decades
down the road without requiring active monitoring/adjusting as the
seasons change and temps swing. Ultimately, I haven't yet come up with
any particularly great setup that I would consider fool-proof over the
span of decades, but I'd be very interested if others have
thoughts/details to share on how this might be achieved.

Conceivably, and to your specific questions John, I'd speculate the
actual controls would at a minimum need to define parameters
establishing a range of "target" loop temperatures for the cooling and
heating season (these would be based on 1st year loop simulations, to
start).  The "melt" loop routed near the surface (or embedded in
concrete where it might be safe), would be normally closed, but valves
would open for circulation when the outside temperatures* are conducive
to pushing the well field return temperatures in the desired direction.
Done cleverly, you could push the system to permit water below the
setpoint during the cooling season, and above the setpoint during the
heating season as well (this behavior would potentially occur in the
swing seasons - spring/fall).  I'm thinking the ideal controls would
constantly monitor and take advantage of free heat rejection & free heat
gains whenever applicable.

* Another point to consider:  What temperatures are ideal to monitor for
such a loop?  Outside air data may be easily available, but considering
the lag in topsoil temps and depending on what you choose for the actual
loop installation location you might do better to locate a sensor
elsewhere... A loop underneath black asphalt getting will experience
different temperatures than the same loop embedded in light-colored
hardscape or simply under exposed topsoil.

Northern climates might find the same setup of interest to primarily
gain free heat gain to keep loops in balance, routing the secondary loop
through building envelopes or similar massed structures that would pick
up extra heat in the summer months.

Suffice to say I find it an interesting topic - if anyone has ever come
across a design guide or white paper on this subject I'd also be very
interested to hear about it!

~Nick

 Job Opportunity


 

 

Building Energy Analysis Software Development Engineer - Syracuse NY

 

Job Description

We are looking for a highly motivated and results driven Building Energy Analysis Software Development Engineer to join the Software Systems group at Carrier Corporation. This position focuses on the development of engineering analysis software such as Carrier E20-II HAP for HVAC system design, whole building energy analysis and LEED Energy and Atmosphere Credit 1 analysis.  Focus also extends to lifecycle cost analysis and HVAC acoustics.  The prospective candidate is expected to grow into a technical leadership role in advancing E20-II  software capabilities and to manage multiple projects to achieve these objectives. In addition, the prospective candidate will also be involved in projects to use modeling software to develop new system architectures and HVAC solutions. 

 

The Software Systems group is responsible for developing and maintaining software-based tools to drive Carrier equipment and systems sales strategy. This includes ownership of Carrier Electronic Catalog Programs (E-CAT) and E20-II/HAP system design tools that are employed by Carrier sales engineers and outside consultants to develop sustainable HVAC system solutions.  This position is based in Syracuse, NY but the candidate will interact with other Carrier team members based in Montluel, France and Shanghai, China.  

 

Specific responsibilities:

?         Work with internal and external customers to identify HVAC analysis needs and develop software tool requirements.

?         Develop physics-based and data-driven models of HVAC systems, equipment and buildings.

?         Develop software designs and specifications.

?         Implement features and models in E20-II software as well as manage software developers to implement features.

?         Participate in software testing and quality assurance.

?         Develop technical documentation.

?         Assist Carrier staff in development of training and software support materials.

?         Assist in delivery of technical support to software users.

?         Team with HVAC systems designers to apply modeling software to develop architectures and solutions to achieve new levels of energy efficiency and system integration.

?         Participate in industry meetings to monitor and influence the HVAC and building modeling community. 

 

 

Education:

Candidates should possess a MS in Mechanical Engineering with at least 7 years experience with concentration in development of computer models for thermodynamic, HVAC and/or power generation systems.  Must be fluent in English (both written and spoken) with excellent technical writing, presentation, and communication skills. Ability to lead and work as a part of a multidisciplinary team in a global environment is required.

 

Experience / Skills:

- 7+ years experience with concentration in development of computer models for thermodynamic, HVAC and/or power generation systems.

- Familiarity with HVAC technologies including air-side systems, water-side systems and equipment such as rooftops, chillers, boilers, water source heat pumps, cooling towers, fans and pumps.

- Experience with HVAC system design, system selection, whole building energy simulation, LEED Energy and Atmosphere Credit 1 analysis and/or green building principles.

- LEED Accredited Professional (AP), BD+C or Green Associate, and/or ASHRAE Building Energy Modeling Professional (BEMP) certification optional but desirable.

- Involvement in industry associations such as ASHRAE or USGBC is a plus.

- Highly motivated, self-directed and able to work independently.

- Passion for excellence and customer service.

- Proficiency with project management

- Experience with software development principles and processes.

- Experience with Microsoft Visual Basic 6.0, Microsoft VB.NET and/or C#.

- Able to effectively manage multiple projects concurrently to meet broad department goals.

- Able to adapt schedule and priorities to meet changing project requirements.

 

Travel: 5%   

 

 

To apply please click here <http://carrier.contacthr.com/20312643> 

 

 

For questions or further information please contact  James.Pegues at carrier.utc.com

 

 

Jim Pegues

From wbishop at pathfinder-ea.com  Mon Jul 25 08:08:19 2011
From: wbishop at pathfinder-ea.com (Bishop, Bill)
Date: Mon, 25 Jul 2011 11:08:19 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt (UNCLASSIFIED)
Message-ID: <247F8F3A32315040A92C035CB2B4E5502D1547@PATHMAIL2.pathfinderengineers.com>

John,

 

While I appreciate the relative novelty of a snow melt system for balancing annual ground loop temperatures, there are a couple ideas that shouldn't be excluded outright when considering server cooling loads: higher cooling temperatures and air-side economizers. The control strategies and equipment for economizers are well-established, and their use is becoming more common in datacenters despite concerns about contaminants and humidity control. A conservative supply air temperature is 75?F, with ASHRAE TC 9.9 recommendation as high as 80.6?F. Google, Intel and Microsoft have all had good experimental results even with 90?F and above, and with little to no humidity control. I also second Carol's suggestion of heat pump space heating and DHW.

 

The servers might have a different opinion:

http://dilbert.com/strips/comic/2011-07-24/

 

Regards,

Bill

 

 

 

From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org [mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Nick Caton
Sent: Friday, July 22, 2011 6:30 PM
To: Carol Gardner; David Eldridge
Cc: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt (UNCLASSIFIED)

 

Hey Carol,

 

In a majority of 4-season climates (here in Kansas City too), I'd expect you would not use such a secondary loop in the dead of summer where night-time temperatures would not drop to practical/conducive levels for heat rejection.

 

But to answer your question from other angles: this heat rejection/heat gain loop would help in the summer months decades down the road, where you've avoided overcooling/overheating the ground temperatures over time.  

 

It would also help in that through late spring and early summer, you would be able to cool through the workday/evening, then use cooler nighttime/morning temperatures to offload the extra heat built up on a daily basis, resulting in cooler net temperatures day-to-day.  This would ultimately reduce and forestall the maximum temperatures that would be reached during mid-to-late summer when you'd want this transfer loop shut off 24/7.

 

I'll be digging into that OSU link David, thanks for the heads up!

 

~Nick

-----Original Message-----
From: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org

[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] On Behalf Of Nick Caton
Sent: Friday, July 22, 2011 4:26 PM
To: Eurek, John S NWO; bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt (UNCLASSIFIED)


I will say I got really excited this past year about the isolated idea
of combining snow melt with geothermal well field design...

For those unfamiliar, well fields in this part of the Midwest (KS/MO/NE)
historically heat up over the long haul, especially with intense
equipment loading like John's situation...  fields installed decades ago
commonly end up struggling and failing when the extreme summers roll
around, sooner or later.

The most common pre-emptive/patch solution I've seen to tackle this
trend is to tack on (or set up infrastructure for) an exterior dry
cooler to reject excess heat from the loop.

I pushed hard recently to consider a "snow melt" loop (even if not used
to functionally melt snow) as a better "free" means of keeping a
well-field in balance annually, but the real trick is in how to set it
up so that this extra loop it doesn't require active
maintenance/monitoring... Making a location for horizontal loops like
this is also a challenge in many cases where the land is not readily
available or has future expansions planned.  Most clients out there,
even those with motivated maintenance staffing, would really be best
served with a "fire and forget" system that could handle itself decades
down the road without requiring active monitoring/adjusting as the
seasons change and temps swing. Ultimately, I haven't yet come up with
any particularly great setup that I would consider fool-proof over the
span of decades, but I'd be very interested if others have
thoughts/details to share on how this might be achieved.

Conceivably, and to your specific questions John, I'd speculate the
actual controls would at a minimum need to define parameters
establishing a range of "target" loop temperatures for the cooling and
heating season (these would be based on 1st year loop simulations, to
start).  The "melt" loop routed near the surface (or embedded in
concrete where it might be safe), would be normally closed, but valves
would open for circulation when the outside temperatures* are conducive
to pushing the well field return temperatures in the desired direction.
Done cleverly, you could push the system to permit water below the
setpoint during the cooling season, and above the setpoint during the
heating season as well (this behavior would potentially occur in the
swing seasons - spring/fall).  I'm thinking the ideal controls would
constantly monitor and take advantage of free heat rejection & free heat
gains whenever applicable.

* Another point to consider:  What temperatures are ideal to monitor for
such a loop?  Outside air data may be easily available, but considering
the lag in topsoil temps and depending on what you choose for the actual
loop installation location you might do better to locate a sensor
elsewhere... A loop underneath black asphalt getting will experience
different temperatures than the same loop embedded in light-colored
hardscape or simply under exposed topsoil.

Northern climates might find the same setup of interest to primarily
gain free heat gain to keep loops in balance, routing the secondary loop
through building envelopes or similar massed structures that would pick
up extra heat in the summer months.

Suffice to say I find it an interesting topic - if anyone has ever come
across a design guide or white paper on this subject I'd also be very
interested to hear about it!

~Nick

From jim at buildingperformanceteam.com  Mon Jul 25 14:13:38 2011
From: jim at buildingperformanceteam.com (Jim Dirkes)
Date: Mon, 25 Jul 2011 17:13:38 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Hotel Renovation and ASHRAE 90.1
Message-ID: <014301cc4b0f$ba003470$2e009d50$@buildingperformanceteam.com>

Dear Forum,

(Excuse cross-posting!)

I'm now modeling a hotel which is seeking LEED certification.  The
"Executive summary" goes like this:

* Lower Level and 1st floor are ~25,000 square feet and contain meeting
rooms, offices, and Lobby.

* Floors 2-12 are guest rooms.

* The building is all-electric, so I'll use Baseline System #2 for guest
rooms and #4 for the remainder.

* The pre-renovation structure was ~80% windows in Climate Zone 2A.

* Post-renovation, the guest rooms floors will be ~45% glass and the
remainder will be ~50% windows.

 

The part I'm uncertain about is how to model the walls and windows. ASHRAE
90.1 Table G3.1, item 5f says 

"f. Existing Buildings. For existing building envelopes, the baseline
building design shall reflect existing conditions prior to any revisions
that are part of the scope of work being evaluated."

That seems to say that the 80% windows becomes the baseline envelope
definition....

On the other hand, the new design will certainly save a LOT of energy!

 

Please let me know your thoughts about:

a) whether the Baseline walls and windows should be modeled "as original"
for their "U" value, SHGF and ....

b) whether the normal 40% maximum glazing applies to an existing building
renovation for either the Baseline or Proposed design.

 

Thanks in advance!

I know I'm not the first one to walk this path, but it's the first time I
have walked it!

 

Description: BPT BizCard JVDII Updated

has, including even happiness.  Love, on the other hand, seeks and rejoices
in all things good for the other.

From cstalberg at naturalintelligence.us  Tue Jul 26 02:48:07 2011
From: cstalberg at naturalintelligence.us (Christian Stalberg)
Date: Tue, 26 Jul 2011 05:48:07 -0400 (EDT)
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Hotel Renovation and ASHRAE 90.1
In-Reply-To: <014301cc4b0f$ba003470$2e009d50$@buildingperformanceteam.com>
Message-ID: <1311673686.17946@naturalintelligence.us>

   When modeling an existing building as the baseline building under
   ASHRAE 90.1 Appendix G you place the fenestration as found (area) and
   original u-values for the envelope including fenestration.

   On 07/25/2011 17:13 Jim Dirkes <jim at buildingperformanceteam.com> wrote
   ..

   Dear Forum,

   (Excuse cross-posting!)

   I'm now modeling a hotel which is seeking LEED certification.  The
   "Executive summary" goes like this:

   * Lower Level and 1st floor are ~25,000 square feet and contain meeting

From shackel at ecw.org  Tue Jul 26 06:42:45 2011
From: shackel at ecw.org (Scott Hackel)
Date: Tue, 26 Jul 2011 08:42:45 -0500
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Snow melt
Message-ID: <912C14F60976CE428135CE5A102DA3C2BE688E355F@SBS.ecw.local>

John -
Seems that the basic goal is to balance both the 1) peak and 2) annual loading on your system more, by finding more heat sinks in both of those timeframes. I think DHW is a great suggestion, as is any measure that lowers your cooling load (economizer, data center efficiency measures, ice storage, higher heat pump efficiency). Adding heating would be the opposite approach - is there any building or process nearby that could benefit from some free heat (multifamily is perfect...)? Beyond that, yes you could consider any type of hybrid geothermal... There are of course many options for supplemental heat rejection, and snow melt is just one of them. If you stick with a hybrid with snow melt as the supplemental device, the thesis by Ramamoorthy (http://www.hvac.okstate.edu/research/Documents/Ramamoorthy_Thesis.pdf ) covers design and control of that particular configuration. And also, if you have access, there was a great presentation by Ed Lohrenz of Geo-Xergy at this summer's ASHRAE Montreal conference that discussed a hybrid with snow melt, I think in Alberta.

Of course, you want to take care in not making the system too complex - more than one supplemental heat sink added on and the system may get tough to control. Controls of basic cooling-dominated hybrid approaches are covered in a couple recent papers: http://www.hvac.okstate.edu/research/Documents/Theses%20and%20reports/Xu_Thesis_2007.pdf and www.ecw.org/hybrid<http://www.ecw.org/hybrid> . These don't deal with snow melt systems though, so they won't answer the question about placement of the control sensor in a snow melt system. Seems to me that what Nick alluded to, putting the sensor in the asphalt or concrete itself, would make the most sense.

Good luck!

Scott Hackel, P.E., LEED AP

Senior Energy Engineer
Energy Center of Wisconsin

455 Science Drive, Suite 200
Madison, WI  53711

www.ecw.org<http://www.ecw.org/>

Hi all!

 

Our problem is, that different reviewers want to see different things.

 

Our first project NC 2.2 Platinum was accepted without any clarification 3
years ago. For the next project, same version we used the same tool, model,
reports ? everything ? we needed two clarifications, had to pay extra money
etc. because of missing results, input files, report structure etc. Based on
this clarified project we did three more whole building simulation were we
got 20 ? 30 comments to clarify. 

 

Projects were all very much the same, so different systems can?t be the
reason. And due to the missing direct communication, it is sometimes hard to
get to know what the reviewer has in mind. 

 

We would appreciate 

-       more details about what is definitely necessary (nobody tells us, if
it is too much information), 

-       what is obsolete,

-       the same reviewer for earch project as far as possible because he is
familiar with the structure of documents etc

-       a way of direct communication after the first review?

 

Does somebody know how much time reviewers have for Minimum/Optimize Energy
Performance / how much money they get?

Von: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org
[mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] Im Auftrag von Nick Caton
Gesendet: Montag, 18. Juli 2011 18:37
An: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Betreff: [Bldg-sim] Suggestions for model reviews

 

Recently, I?ve had an odd string of personal contacts and strangers alike
confiding with me and demonstrating their frustrations over energy model
reviews.  As an outside-party, it?s actually largely entertaining to hear,
and puts a few things in perspective.  We are all human, and we all share
common emotions when put under the spotlight, but how we put those feelings
to action makes all the difference between an easy/productive review process
and a train wreck?

 

I?ve had the pleasure of treading both paths, and I thought it might be
helpful for modelers of all skill/experience levels to share a few tips I?ve
developed to manage the stress-level in your energy model reviews.  A few
reviewers out there might also benefit to turn this advice around and apply
it to their practices as well:

 

1.       Do not assume your reviewer knows less than you.  

a.       Recognize that stuff gets lost in translation ? energy modeling
minutiae are rarely easy to communicate in text format.

b.      It?s human to feel annoyed when your work is questioned, but practice
patience.  Odds are you and/or your reviewer are going to learn something as
a result. 

c.       If you ever catch yourself thinking/responding in a dismissive or
condescending fashion, stop.  Your heart is in the wrong place (however right
you might be) and you?re missing the point of an objective review.  Take a
breather!

2.       Do not assume your reviewer knows more than you, either.  

a.       Do not be afraid to challenge or question a query, but prepare and
present a solid case for any contrary position.  

b.      It is very easy to run yourself in circles trying to explain/justify
yourself when it isn?t really necessary.  

c.       Reviewers can and should make it plain when an in-depth explanation
is required.

3.       Be prepared to change your mind!  For better or for worse, reviews
are ultimately beneficial to all parties involved, because they provide an
excellent opportunity to learn a thing or two.  Allow your personal expertise
to continually evolve and incorporate, or at least recognize, others?
perspectives and practices.  They may serve you well personally in the
future!

4.       Once in a while, you may have to work with an individual who is
truly stubborn, unreasonable and is not willing to participate in any
objective discussion.  If a review should fail to ?close out? over such
behavior, the best you can hope for is to document communications so you can
later make apparent in hindsight that you were proactive, and pulling your
weight to move the process forward.  

 

If anyone has similar guiding principles or tips to share (from either side
of the reviewing fence), I think it would be much appreciated =)!

 

~Nick

 

 

 

NICK CATON, P.E.

SENIOR ENGINEER

 

Smith & Boucher Engineers

25501 west valley parkway, suite 200

olathe, ks 66061

direct 913.344.0036

fax 913.345.0617

www.smithboucher.com

Dear Forum,
(Excuse cross-posting!)
I'm now modeling a hotel which is seeking LEED certification.  The "Executive summary" goes like this:
* Lower Level and 1st floor are ~25,000 square feet and contain meeting rooms, offices, and Lobby.
* Floors 2-12 are guest rooms.
* The building is all-electric, so I'll use Baseline System #2 for guest rooms and #4 for the remainder.
* The pre-renovation structure was ~80% windows in Climate Zone 2A.
* Post-renovation, the guest rooms floors will be ~45% glass and the remainder will be ~50% windows.

The part I'm uncertain about is how to model the walls and windows. ASHRAE 90.1 Table G3.1, item 5f says
"f. Existing Buildings. For existing building envelopes, the baseline building design shall reflect existing conditions prior to any revisions that are part of the scope of work being evaluated."
That seems to say that the 80% windows becomes the baseline envelope definition....
On the other hand, the new design will certainly save a LOT of energy!

Please let me know your thoughts about:
a) whether the Baseline walls and windows should be modeled "as original" for their "U" value, SHGF and ....
b) whether the normal 40% maximum glazing applies to an existing building renovation for either the Baseline or Proposed design.

Thanks in advance!
I know I'm not the first one to walk this path, but it's the first time I have walked it!

From cholekar at gmail.com  Wed Jul 27 01:49:12 2011
From: cholekar at gmail.com (=?UTF-8?B?U2FuamF5IOCkuOCkguCknOCkryBDaG9sZWthcg==?=)

Subject: [Bldg-sim] Custum curves
In-Reply-To: <CAHpz=HR-rb8CC2dm2OpbTKGz_81DYLMWgd7u3Qy2Y0ZWa6K6Qg@mail.gmail.com>
References: <CAHpz=HR-rb8CC2dm2OpbTKGz_81DYLMWgd7u3Qy2Y0ZWa6K6Qg@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <CAH1fBWwZ79UJGotFPP5xeXaj3U_SvoNuvqeP=T0o4J91QOWpJg@mail.gmail.com>

A resistance heater, converting 100% of its input electricity to output heat
would have a *3.41214 EER* (COP = 1), due to the fact that it takes about
0.293071 watt hours <http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Watt_hours> to equal one
BTU <http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BTU>.
Take* EER = COP x 3.412*

These curves are better explained in iesVE Software.

Regards,
Sanjay Cholekar

Hi Deepak

I think you can specify destratification fans so that would essentially be the same. Other than that you could try splitting the zone in 2 and letting internal air transfer between the zones work out the exchange but that's not really the ideal way to do it.

Otherwise you probably need to think CFD and you'd be in the realms of moving mesh transient CFD then and I wouldn't recommend that.

Not an easy one to solve really.

Paul

if E+ reports Fanger PPD or PMV but you could do this in ESP-r by cranking up the air
velocity used for comfort calculations.  And impose high heat transfer for convection
either via fixed heat transfer coefficients or a fan hc regime.  This might allow you
to avoid CFD.

From timothy.moore at iesve.com  Wed Jul 27 11:38:22 2011
From: timothy.moore at iesve.com (Timothy Moore)
Date: Wed, 27 Jul 2011 19:38:22 +0100
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Modelling of ceiling fan
In-Reply-To: <2C3CF455489D6B4F95AFAB84847FA4130194AA0A781D@E2K7-MS2.ds.strath.ac.uk>
References: <20110727104140.66C408DB0028@zapata.dreamhost.com>
	<2C3CF455489D6B4F95AFAB84847FA4130194AA0A781D@E2K7-MS2.ds.strath.ac.uk>
Message-ID: <7E4EF7766063EC4091542B5B3E671EC403E31281@MAIL.iesve.com>

Deepak ,

 

If your focus is indeed thermal comfort, within IES VE you can, if
desired, adjust heat transfer coefficients for surfaces, which will
influence radiant comfort, and in the results viewer adjust the air
velocity for thermal comfort analysis. 

 

If you wish to look at any one simulation time step in more detail, you
can then export the boundary conditions, etc. to the CFD module in the
VE and look at local air velocities and thermal comfort in a space with
the ceiling fan, or at least a reasonable approximation of such a fan. 

 

If you wish to look at the energy saving potential for ceiling fans, you
may first want to study the thermal comfort implications with respect to
room temperature set points (i.e., can you use a higher cooling set
point to achieve the desired level of thermal comfort with the ceiling
fan). Having done so, you can then run the energy model with adjusted
set points to determine energy savings and re-check the thermal comfort
analysis to ensure that the design requirements throughout the simulated
time period will be met with the adjusted set points plus ceiling fans. 

 

Regards,

Timothy

 

 

  <http://www.iesve.com/>

From yjhuang at whiteboxtechnologies.com  Wed Jul 27 11:48:27 2011
From: yjhuang at whiteboxtechnologies.com (Joe Huang)
Date: Wed, 27 Jul 2011 11:48:27 -0700
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Modelling of ceiling fan
In-Reply-To: <20110727104140.66C408DB0028@zapata.dreamhost.com>
References: <20110727104140.66C408DB0028@zapata.dreamhost.com>
Message-ID: <4E305D7B.4070306@whiteboxtechnologies.com>

I think this level of detail in modeling is unnecessary. In fact, I don't think there's 
need for any enhancements to the thermal modeling
of the building to assess the comfort benefits of ceiling fans.   Ceiling fans do not 
change the thermal conditions of the space (except for
a little added heat generated by the fan motor).  It provides comfort through air motion 
experienced by the occupants.   The improved
comfort can be quantified explicitly using Fanger's equation,  or implicitly if you 
calculate an equivalent temperature to that with still air.

From: Hopf, Johannes <johannes.hopf at dreso.com>
Subject: Re: [Bldg-sim] Suggestions for model reviews

Hi all! ?Our problem is, that different reviewers want to see different things. ?Our first project NC 2.2 Platinum was accepted without any clarification 3 years ago. For the next project, same version we used the same tool, model, reports ? everything ? we needed two clarifications, had to pay extra money etc. because of missing results, input files, report structure etc. Based on this clarified project we did three more whole building simulation were we got 20 ? 30 comments to clarify.  ?Projects were all very much the same, so different systems can?t be the reason. And due to the missing direct communication, it is sometimes hard to get to know what the reviewer has in mind.  ?We would appreciate -?????? more details about what is definitely necessary (nobody tells us, if it is too much information), -?????? what is obsolete,-?????? the same reviewer for earch project as far as possible because he is familiar with the
 structure of documents etc-?????? a way of direct communication after the first review? ?Does somebody know how much time reviewers have for Minimum/Optimize Energy Performance / how much money they get? ?Regards Johannes ?Von: bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org [mailto:bldg-sim-bounces at lists.onebuilding.org] Im Auftrag von Nick Caton
Gesendet: Montag, 18. Juli 2011 18:37
An: bldg-sim at lists.onebuilding.org
Betreff: [Bldg-sim] Suggestions for model reviews ?Recently, I?ve had an odd string of personal contacts and strangers alike confiding with me and demonstrating their frustrations over energy model reviews.? As an outside-party, it?s actually largely entertaining to hear, and puts a few things in perspective.? We are all human, and we all share common emotions when put under the spotlight, but how we put those feelings to action makes all the difference between an easy/productive review process and a train wreck? ?I?ve had the pleasure of treading both paths, and I thought it might be helpful for modelers of all skill/experience levels to share a few tips I?ve developed to manage the stress-level in your energy model reviews.? A few reviewers out there might also benefit to turn this advice around and apply it to their practices as well: ?1.?????? Do not assume your reviewer knows less than you. ?a.?????? Recognize that stuff
 gets lost in translation ? energy modeling minutiae are rarely easy to communicate in text format.b.????? It?s human to feel annoyed when your work is questioned, but practice patience.? Odds are you and/or your reviewer are going to learn something as a result. c.?????? If you ever catch yourself thinking/responding in a dismissive or condescending fashion, stop.? Your heart is in the wrong place (however right you might be) and you?re missing the point of an objective review.? Take a breather!2.?????? Do not assume your reviewer knows more than you, either.? a.?????? Do not be afraid to challenge or question a query, but prepare and present a solid case for any contrary position.? b.????? It is very easy to run yourself in circles trying to explain/justify yourself when it isn?t really necessary. ?c.?????? Reviewers can and should make it plain when an in-depth explanation is required.3.?????? Be
 prepared to change your mind!? For better or for worse, reviews are ultimately beneficial to all parties involved, because they provide an excellent opportunity to learn a thing or two. ?Allow your personal expertise to continually evolve and incorporate, or at least recognize, others? perspectives and practices.? They may serve you well personally in the future!4.?????? Once in a while, you may have to work with an individual who is truly stubborn, unreasonable and is not willing to participate in any objective discussion.? If a review should fail to ?close out? over such behavior, the best you can hope for is to document communications so you can later make apparent in hindsight that you were proactive, and pulling your weight to move the process forward.?  ?If anyone has similar guiding principles or tips to share (from either side of the reviewing fence), I think it would be much appreciated =)! ?~Nick ? ?NICK CATON, P.E.SENIOR
 ENGINEER ?Smith & Boucher Engineers25501 west valley parkway, suite 200olathe, ks 66061direct 913.344.0036fax 913.345.0617www.smithboucher.com  ?

From hisham at edsglobal.com  Wed Jul 27 23:43:47 2011
From: hisham at edsglobal.com (Hisham Ahmad)
Date: Thu, 28 Jul 2011 12:13:47 +0530
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Modelling of ceiling fan
In-Reply-To: <OF22B69284.EAEDD3BE-ON652578DA.00291DAF-652578DA.00299A16@teri.res.in>
References: <014301cc4b0f$ba003470$2e009d50$@buildingperformanceteam.com>
	<7F7FF64673C26D4DA4A62094657BD9140C2430F9F4@wattsexch.wattsae.local>
	<OF22B69284.EAEDD3BE-ON652578DA.00291DAF-652578DA.00299A16@teri.res.in>
Message-ID: <CA+qGebMei9O+ucR5aCJxo==UKWQwY65gAVz6Nr4Zttv-zAYpgg@mail.gmail.com>

Hi,

We recently modeled the effect of ceiling fans by estimating the airflow
(based on rpm, diameter & manufacturer data etc) that the fan would create
and corelating it with the guidelines of ASHRAE 55 for increased airflow.
We used a higher cooling set point to compute the energy savings for fans
following the exact methodology as pointed out by Mr Moore in this thread. The
fan power was added to the model as an equipment load. However, this was
done in DOE2.

On 27 July 2011 13:04, Deepak Tewari <deepak.tewari at teri.res.in> wrote:

Dear Paulo,

You may want to take a look at DesignBuilder. It is a program that I have
started to use and am quite happy with its effective graphic user interface
as well as its visual delivery of results. It uses DOE's Energy+ program in
its algorithms which is a well established software on its own. There is a
month long free trial plus helpful online tutorials on how to use the
program's features.
Perhaps spending some time on this program may help you get a better feel on
what the advantages and limitations of these simulations are.

Regards,

Ali Yaras

BREEAM Int. / Energy Specialist

INNOVATIV projekteringsledning Sverige AB

From kajalm at gmail.com  Sun Jul 31 09:49:04 2011
From: kajalm at gmail.com (Kajal Chatterjee)
Date: Sun, 31 Jul 2011 12:49:04 -0400
Subject: [Bldg-sim] Energy Analyst/Energy Modeler - Job Announcement
Message-ID: <CAEQu9fnAPug1ty6kwU4d4UpJ2gVx=UjciZ=u+DohjKq+XnVUzw@mail.gmail.com>

Position available in SWA's New York/Connecticut Office -

Energy Analyst/Energy Modeler:
Steven Winter Associates, Inc. (SWA) seeks a qualified professional to
provide energy analysis for high performance and green buildings, including
multifamily, commercial, and institutional buildings. Work would primarily
focus on energy modeling in eQUEST/Energy Plus. Energy modeling would
support LEED (Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design) projects
nationally, NYSERDA Multifamily Program or New Construction Program projects
and LIPA projects in New York. Depending on experience and interest, other
opportunities may be available in energy auditing services and building
performance diagnostics. Opening may be available in SWA?s New York or
Connecticut offices.

Summary of essential job function:
-Enthusiasm for high performance buildings
-Ability to perform energy modeling

systems
-Knowledge of energy efficiency principles and ASHRAE 90.1-2007
-Aptitude for writing and presenting technical concepts to project team
members
-Ability to organize and run several projects at once
Minimum requirements:
Experience or familiarity with modeling software tools (eQUEST/Energy Plus)
is highly desirable, but detailed training will be provided. Experience or
knowledge of other modeling tools is beneficial, but not required. Candidate
should have experience with high performance buildings and a willingness to
learn. Compensation is commensurate with experience.
